(b)      Second, the  Project will have  important institutional benefits, through
strengthening indigenous capacities to target, appraise and monitor efforts to
reduce poverty. This will not only be in the TASIF, but also in the national and
local govermnents, and in local non-government organizations.  In addition, the



- 38 -
Project will contribute to community strengthening through the increased involve-
ment and local ownership of development activities.
(c)    Third, the Project will set the stage for a larger program of poverty allevi-
ation in the future, modified as appropriate on the basis of the experience of the
pilot, and assist the Government in trying to attract additional financing.
B. Risks
6.2    There are three main risks associated with the Project. These are as follows:
(a)    First, the security situation in much of the country is fragile, and there is
still some fighting in the East. To reduce this risk, the first component of the
Project has been designed to support the extension of poverty alleviation activities
in areas where programs are already successfully underway. Expansion or replica-
tion of these or other programs and the microprojects financed by the TASIF will
be undertaken only where the situation is sufficiently stable.
(b)    Second, political factors could compromise the autonomy, credibility and
effectiveness of the programs supported by the international non-governmental
organizations and the newly established TASIF. The risk to the former will be
minimized through regular cooperation between the non-government organizations
and the TASIF staff. This cooperation will be facilitated by technical assistance
provided through the Project. This risk will also be reduced by detailing the roles
and responsibilities of the Government and the NGOs in individual contracts, and
monitoring fulfillment of obligations through the Project's monitoring and evalua-
tion system and regular Bank supervision. Another risk is interference in daily
operations which will compromise the TASIF's credibility and effectiveness to ap-
prove microprojects in an unbiased and rapid manner. To reduce these risks, the
TASIF is being established as a semi-autonomous body, with a Board of non-gov-
ernmental and governmental members and chaired by the Prime Minister. This ar-
rangement should create a high profile and broad ownership, and thus help protect
the integrity of the TASIF. There will also be comprehensive institutional checks
and balances, and efficient operating procedures detailed in the operational man-
ual, which will be checked closely during project supervision.
(c)    Third, there are risks associated with the limited managerial, technical and
institutional capacity of local organizations responsible for proposing and imple-
menting microprojects, and for financing the operation and maintenance of the
microprojects after completion. To reduce these risks, the appraisal procedures for
the microprojects will include careful analysis of institutional capacity, and techni-
cal assistance will be provided to fill institutional, technical and management gaps.
Furthermore, increased community participation in the identification and imple-
mentation of the microprojects will increase the likelihood that microprojects
respond to urgent community needs, thus increasing the likelihood for sustaining
investments.



- 39 -
VII. AGREEMENTS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
A. Conditions and Agreements
7.1   As a condition of Credit effectiveness:
-the Government will have deposited its initial contribution equivalent to
US$ 75,000 in the project account;
7.2    Dated covenants:
By December 31, 1997 the TASIF will have developed a targeting strategy satis-
factory to IDA to guide distribution of resources of the rest of the Project duration
(para 4.18).
By December 31, 1997 the TASIF will have submitted to IDA a proposal for the
use of the future sponsored programs funds (para 4.14).
By June 30, 1998 the TASIF will have carried out the mid-term project review
(para 5.27)
7.3    General conditions.
(a)    Agreement for World Bank procurement procedures to be used by the
TASIF for its own procurement.
(b)    Prior review of microprojects over $50,000
(c)   Implementation completion report.
B. Recommendation
7.7   Subject to the above conditions, the proposed project will constitute a suitable
basis for an IDA credit of SDR 8.7 million (US$12 million equivalent) to the Republic of
Tajikistan, to be repaid on standard IDA terms with 40 years maturity, including 10 years
of grace at standard IDA terms.

REPUBLIC OF TAJIKISTAN
PILOT POVERTY ALLEVIATION PROJECT
SHELTER AND HOUSE REHABILITATION PROGRAM (SAVE THE CHILDREN-US)
SUMMARY
1.    The proposed two-and-a-half year project will alleviate poverty through assisting 2000 returned
families in the self-help reconstruction of their homes in five districts most devastated by the recent civil
war.  This is 37% of the identified need. 420 tonnes of food (wheat flour, beans and oil) will be
provided to over 100 work groups each of 15-20 persons, representing an estimated 210,000 days of
labor. At least 2000 other families are expected to benefit through reduction of overcrowding, double
and triple family occupancy of single homes being common. The project will also promote the adoption
of environmentally sound roofing technology ie. use of fiber-cement tiles in place of asbestos roofing
sheets. Local communities will be involved in beneficiary identification, monitoring of food distribution
and construction, and in the identification of subsequent priorities in poverty alleviation. The project will
be self-targeting, with controls to ensure the inclusion of the neediest beneficiaries and to prevent
misallocation of inputs. The project will operate in the priority need districts areas of: Bokhtar, Vakhsh,
Kolkhozabad, Kabodien and Shaartuz districts in the Khatlon region of Tajikistan. The project would
be implemented by SCF-US Tajikistan and would have a budget of $3.5 million.
OBJECTIVES
2.    Much of the devastation during the 1992-3 civil war was concentrated in two areas of Western
Khatlon Region. In some instances entire villages were razed. Although UNHCR and SCF-US have
assisted many thousands to rebuild their homes, there remains a backlog of over 6,000 identified families
whose houses are not rebuilt, the most needy districts being Bokhtar (2,000 houses), Vakhsh (1,700)
Kabodien and Shaartuz (750 each) and Kolkhozabad (400). These numbers are increasing as more
families return to their ruined homes. Most of these families cannot feed themselves or satisfy basic
health and education needs, let alone finance reconstruction. Even if they had the resources, locally
available roofing materials are lacking. Basic shelter (2-3 rooms) is a fundamental precondition for
economic rehabilitation, which can be carried out through other means (development of household plots,
income generation activities, employment creation, re-establishment of social service infrastructure).
Therefore it is not surprising that the social assessment of the Bank identified housing needs as a critical
priority of the poor.
3.    Compounding the situation is massive unemployment and underemployment; factories and
enterprises are non-functioning, the micro-economies of the kolkhozes and sovkhozes is in advanced
decline, and workers have either been laid off or receive no salaries, or partial and irregular salaries.
Skilled craftsmen, including carpenters, builders, mechanics, are largely idle or have left Tajikistan to
find employment elsewhere in the CIS. Finally, there is a chronic and serious food deficit. On a
different level, there is a pressing need to reestablish security, law and order, community cohesion, and



- 42 -
an atmosphere of trust and cooperation between local communities and local governments and institutions,
so that people can begin to resume normal lives. The cramped, destitute living conditions of the
populations can only serve to create or exacerbate local tensions.
4.    The project would aim to alleviate poverty by satisfying a significant portion (37 percent) of the
identified housing needs within two years, creating employment through seasonal local work groups, and
fostering community cooperation and solidarity through such neighborhood associations. The project
would also temporarily relieve the food deficit, pending an eventual rise in local food production, and
support local entrepreneurs and craftsmen in the promotion of an environmentally-sound technology: the
production and use of fiber-cement tiles.
5.    Specific objectives would be that, by the end of two years (March 1997 to March 1999):
(a)    Two thousand needy homeless families would have reconstructed basic shelter (kitchen,
living room/bedroom) with assistance from local Food For Work (FFW) brigades.
(b)    Local community FFW brigades would have carried out reconstruction activities for 2000
houses, and improved their families' food security through the estimated 420 MT of food
(wheat flour, beans, oil) which would be distributed as compensation for approximately
210,000 days equivalent of labor (note: FFW calculations are based on reconstruction
completed, not time spent).
(c)    The demonstrated practice of community participation in reconstruction, needs
identification and monitoring would have produced measurable positive changes in
community attitudes towards defining and meeting local development needs, both in the
project area and in other parts of the country (indirect benefit).
(d)    The successful use of fiber-cement roofing tiles would have supported the expansion of
an environmentally-friendly technology, the creation a cadre of skilled workers, both
within the program area and in other parts of the country (indirect benefit).
STRATEGY AND TARGETING
6.    The strategy would aim to alleviate poverty through supporting community self-help efforts to
create the physical and social infrastructure that will enable a community to address more long-term
needs. The area selected contains a majority of returned refugees and IDPs who need to be reintegrated
into local communities. Further direct targeting of beneficiaries would be undertaken during the project
to ensure that benefits will be targeted to the neediest families.  The project beneficiaries would be
primarily, although not necessarily exclusively, the neediest families with many small children, widows,
families caring for orphans and pensioners, and would include an equitable proportion of women-headed
households. Currently, approximately 30 per cent of the brigade members are women, many brigades
have a majority of women members, and some brigades are entirely composed of women. Similarly all
efforts have been made to ensure mixed ethnicity within the brigades. Emphasis is placed on this being
a community project, with equal participation from all elements of the community.



- 43 -
7.     The FFW element is designed to relieve food deficits, and to foster local cooperation in shelter
reconstruction. The donated supply of basic construction materials, beyond the means of poor families
to acquire, is supplemented by a significant local contribution in labor and in finishing. Work groups
make use of local skills.
8.     The above strategy has been shown to be a popular and effective way to meet several needs
simultaneously: reinforced community organization and participation, shortage of construction materials,
and shortage of food and employment opportunities, particularly for unemployed skilled artisans and
unskilled labor. The seasonal work program meshes well with off-season work in canal clearing. The
specific steps to implement the strategy, explained further below under the section on implementation,
are:
(a)    Extensive village meetings are held to inform people of the project, undertake social
mapping, identify eligible and needy beneficiaries and particularly ensure the inclusion
of women, calculate FFW and material requirements;
(b)    Village work brigades are formed and a work plan prepared;
(c)    Materials and food are procured, and their delivery and utilization monitored both by the
local representatives and the NGO.
9.     Two new elements have been included in the strategy of this project in order to improve
effectiveness and coverage.
(a)    As a sub-component of the project a subcontractor would supply tiels and train local
carpenters and artisans in roof construction (which is slightly different from that required
for asbestos sheeting), quality control and repairs.  In order to demonstrate the
technology and determine training needs, three demonstration houses would be roofed
with tiles prior to project effectiveness. The use of roofing tiles in place of the
previously-used asbestos sheets would remove a potential health hazard, and support
additional employment that would disseminate this technology more widely.
(b)    Site visits and training will be arranged for a small number of selected community
representatives and government officials from other parts of the country where there is
an identified housing need (rural Penjikent, Aini, Mascho), with a view to building
capacity in other local organizations for similar programs. The addition of a small
training and extension component will enable the methodology to be adopted and adapted
to the needs of other areas where SCF-US does not work, and where similar housing
needs of the very poor exist for reasons other than destruction in civil war.



- 44 -
ACTIVITIES
10.   Extensive village meetings would be held to inform people of the program modalities and its
limitations. Direct selection of the neediest areas is done initially from an already-prepared list of
beneficiaries, through community-based participatory social mapping, and consultation with other NGOs
working in the area and key community resource people (particularly informal leaders, neighborhood
(mahallah) community representatives) and local SCF-US staff to identify the neediest women-headed
households. These mechanisms will identify the neighborhoods of greatest destruction and/or poverty.
SCF-US organizers will work in pairs of one man and one woman, to promote women's participation and
assess the volume of reconstruction work (number of houses multiplied by the estimated percentage of
destruction). These organizers will also ensure, through spot visits and other means, that the beneficiaries
are indeed village residents, and that they have not received any free construction materials in the past
three years.
11.    SCF-US staff then calculate the food and material requirements. The material requirements are
calculated according to the annexed Technical Note 1, and the food requirements according to the
annexed Technical Note 2.
12.   Village brigades are formed (usually 15-20 persons), from the community members. Each
brigade elects a leader, and a detailed contract is formalized between SCF-US and the brigades (including
terms and conditions, details of each house owner and work to be done, and names of the brigade
members). Usually the brigade leader enters into a contract with the village executive committee for the
provision of transport. The establishing of such a relationship is hoped to encourage the extension of
official participation to include further negotiated contributions from their own resources towards minor
repair works of community benefit (e.g. repair of street lights).
13.    Roofing materials (tiles and timber) will be procured by International Competitive Bidding, except
for the requirements for the first two hundred houses which will be procured by Direct Contract and
International Shopping respectively. The materials will remain under SCF-US control from receipt to
delivery upon the brigade's completion of preparatory reconstruction work (to wall-plate level). The
delivery of the materials and food is authorized upon receipt of a satisfactory post-survey report prepared
by SCF-US. The brigade is responsible for organizing the transport and distribution of the items to the
respective beneficiaries. This process is rigorously monitored and documented fully by SCF-US.
14.   Food is released and distributed according to specified procedures against work done (historical
average is 210 kg. of food per house, consisting of 90 kg. wheat flour, 90 kg. beans, and 30 kg. edible
oil). House construction is seasonal (March to November), and food distributed for completed housing
is targeted to reach the beneficiaries during the most critical pre-harvest period of food shortages. (Note:
SCF-US's FFW activities continue for canal cleaning and war rubble clearance during the non-
construction period of the year under a program financed by USDA independently of this Shelter
program.
15.   In order to demonstrate the technology and determine training needs, three demonstration houses
were roofed with tiles prior to project start-up, to demonstrate the effectiveness of the tiles. Site visits
and training will be arranged for a small number of selected community representatives and government
officials from other parts of the country where there is an identified housing need (i.e. rural Penjikent,
Aini, Mascho), with a view to building capacity in other local organizations for similar programs.



- 45 -
16.    The schedule of activities is listed in Table 1. The following notes explain more precisely what
is the content of such activities, if not clarified above. (a) Hire staff: the staffing requirements, local and
international, are detailed under management below. Local staff are hired on contract for the duration
of the project; (b) Train staff: staff will be trained in the SCF-US methodology, including social
mapping; (c) Social mapping: this is an additional activity to ensure that the lists provided by officials
are objective identifications of poverty areas and poorer households. The maps will be prepared in
participatory fashion, and used for subsequent monitoring and programming; (d) Post survey: normally
it takes between three weeks to one month to construct a house, and brigades usually work on several
houses at a time. The post survey is a verification by SCF-US staff that the construction has been
satisfactorily completed, and is the basis for approval of distribution of commodities.

MANAGEMENT
17.   Overall management of the project would be the responsibility of the SCF-US Field Office
Director (FOD) in Tajikistan. The Program Officer (1) is responsible for overall monitoring and
reporting. Two Supervisors would collect and finalize the lists of needy families, identify the most
vulnerable, and supervise project implementation in the field. Ten Organizers are responsible for
monitoring the progress of construction and for supervising food and materials distribution. Necessary
support staff include: accountants (2), data entry clerks (3), computer specialist (1), secretaries (3),
logistics assistants (2), store keeper (1), warehouse keepers (2); drivers (2), guards (12), and cleaners (3).
Total full-time national staff costs are less than 6 percent of the entire project costs (excluding value of
food and local contributions).
18.   The project requires the part-time support of international staff whose costs are charged to the
project in proportion to their time allocation (time sheets are kept). The Field Office Director (FOD)
(20%), assisted by the Deputy FOD/Program (35%), is responsible for overall performance of the
project, delegation of duties, recruitment, performance and discipline of staff, and reporting to donors
and the Government of Tajikistan. The Deputy FOD/Administration and Finance (10%) is responsible
for financial management, monitoring, reporting and procurement. The Program Director (40%) and
Program Manager (20%) are responsible for the actual implementation and for all data management
requirements of the project, and for the MIS (already developed).
19.   SCF-US has operated in Tajikistan since 1994 under an agreement with the government and in
close cooperation with USDA, USAID and UNHCR. The proposed project would build on an area in
which capacity and experience has already been demonstrated. With over 160 staff, the organization has
mobilized over 18,000 people to rebuild their villages, and has ensured targeting of women and
accountability of transactions at village level.
ENVIRONMENTAL ASPECTS
20.   The project rehabilitates ruined structures in existing habitations, where families are returning to
their former houses. The result is significant improvement in the built environment, with no adverse
impacts on the natural environment.
SUSTAINABILITY
21.   Sustainability of impact is built into the project, as the significant improvements to shelter would
remove a key constraint to income generation for poor families and enable them to concentrate on
productive investment of time; it would also have a positive impact on family health and therefore on
productivity by reducing overcrowding and improving living conditions.
22.   Sustainability of process is limited. The project strategy is a "rehabilitation" mode, mid-way
between relief and development, and is facilitated by external provision of food for work and building
materials. In some cases work groups have continued on their own dynamic, and they often participate
in further poverty alleviation and reconstruction activities (e.g. Group Guaranteed Lending, canal cleaning



- 47 -
and rubble clearance). but for the foreseeable future, until there is a substantial increase in local food
production and local security, most are likely to continue to rely on external inputs for some years to
come. The activity of reconstruction in Tajikistan depends almost entirely on external provision of food
for work, and scarce reconstruction materials. Unless the project continues, the remaining deficit of
4,000 houses, together with those of newly returned families, will only very gradually be rehabilitated.
23.    Outside the project area, the express aim would be to generate a sustained impact of process
independently of the project, although links with TASIF would be required to sustain this process on any
significant scale, especially in poor and marginal areas.
LINKS WITH TASIF
24.    It is expected that the work groups and village committees would  be informed  through this
Project and encouraged to develop proposals for co-financing self-help micro-projects. The training
and extension component is designed explicitly to generate capacity in other regions (e.g. eastern
mountains, Leninabad districts).

25.    The budget above summarizes main line items by source. The total costs of the project would
include the following:
(a) Community Contribution. Community  contribution  in labor for actual reconstruction work
is compensated through the FFW mechanism, which is not less than the local prevailing daily
rate for unskilled labor. In addition to the food compensation, the community/house owner will
contribute in terms labor and locally available material. Such contribution is estimated to be
$188,000 for the whole project. Community contribution is estimated to be nine labor-days for
roofing and 35 labor-days for finishing the house. Material contribution from the house owner,
for the ceiling, windows and doors, is estimated to be $50 per house. The community and the



- 48 -
house owners also contribute in many other ways, the total value of which is difficult to cost:
(i) selection of beneficiaries, social mapping, monitoring; (ii) negotiation with village authorities
for transport and other requirements: (iii) distribution of materials and food (under supervision).
(b) Village Executive Committees: The Village Executive Committees (grassroots level government
structure responsible for development activities in the village) are responsible for providing SCF-
US with lists of potential beneficiaries, and assist in carrying out surveys, calculating
distributions, and providing transport for the food and materials from SCF-US warehouses to the
community.
(c) USDA. USDA will provide 420 MT of food commodities, with a local market value of
approximately $261,600 (based on July 1996 market prices), and $131,760 for associated
handling costs. The contribution from USDA is calculated as a proportion of food to be used in
this project. Food calculation is based on SCF-US's existing norms of 210 kg. per house,
assuming an average of 75 % destruction.
MONITORING AND REPORTING
26.    Key indicators would be monitored by the Program Officer with participation from brigades. All
key indicators would be monitored monthly and reported quarterly.
27.    Quantitative
(a) Beneficiaries/poverty monitoring: number of reconstructed houses benefiting (i) families with
many children; (ii) widows; (iii) families with orphans; (iv) pensioners; (v) female-headed
households;
(b) Number/proportion of women participating in construction brigades: (monthly during construction
period);
(c) Number of houses reconstructed;
(d) Quantities of construction material distributed;
(e) Quantities of food distributed, and approximate labor days equivalent.
(f) Using information collected from points (c)-(e), determine the average inputs per house
constructed (used for forward planning, costing, and efficiency control). In addition, monitoring
is carried out by random unannounced visits.
Oualitative
(g) Construction quality and load-bearing capacity of walls and roofing must be adequate for tiles,
a material whose use is not well-known locally. Special efforts will be made by SCF-US and SNI
to ensure that all structures are adequate, that standards are maintained, and that roofing
performance under different conditions (rain, snow, high winds) is assured.



- 49 -
(h) A simple assessment of the contribution of housing to poverty alleviation (household standard of
living) will be undertaken, through post-reconstruction visits and individual/group interviews.
Local communities will be involved in this component.
Key Objective
(i) The program will also participate in the Project-wide monitoring of key objective indicators that
will measure progress toward the major objectives poverty impact and institutional capacity.
28.   Implementation, monitoring and financial reports will be forwarded quarterly by the FOD to the
officer designated by GOT. Quarterly reports will assess progress against targets, identify any reasons
for exceeding or shortfall of targets, and indicate consequent action already taken or to be taken. In case
of significant change to the implementation schedule, exceptional reports shall also be submitted to GOT.
INTEGRATED EVALUATION
29.   An independent integrated evaluation at mid-term and project completion would be contracted by
TASIF to be undertaken by a consultant leading a multi-disciplinary team of local and international
experts. Implementing INGOs including SCF-US would each nominate a team member, but the team as
a whole would evaluate all aspects of both components of the project including the SCF-US Shelter
program. The team would visit project sites, review reports and indicator measurements in the light of
its own observations and investigations, and seek to establish lessons to be learned both at mid-term and
for Project follow-up. It would report both to TASIF and to the Bank. Further details are in Annex L.
30.   Key objective indicators will be measured and monitored by the Project in order to track, in a
consistent and comparable fashion, the essentials of performance against objectives. The key objective
indicators are summarized in Table 1 of Annex L. The first Project objective of impact on the incomes
of the poor will be measured across all sub-components by five key indicators, of which two will indicate
the extent to which this objective has been met in a cost effective and participatory fashion. In addition,
each sub-component will be evaluated according to three specific indicators relating to the particular
program. For the Shelter program these three specific indicators are: shelter; vulnerable groups
benefiting; and technology adoption (tiles). These indicators measure impact, coverage and sustainability.
The second Project objective of capability building would be measured by two general indicators across
all components, with a third relating specifically to the training of TASIF staff. Thus each sub-
component will be evaluated on ten indicators, of which seven are common to all sub-components of the
Project, and three are specific to each sub-component.  These indicators, and the specific measures and
instruments to compile them, would be refined in a pilot baseline exercise.
31.   Monitoring and evaluation activities have already been a prominent feature of the existing NGO
Shelter program, and both the findings and systems used will be built upon in the scaled-up program.
The Project would closely monitor the scaled-up program. Specifically, SCF-US would be asked to
report to the TASIF on a quarterly basis on the following: (i) summary of achievements: number of
beneficiaries reached (direct and indirect) versus goal; cost per beneficiary (total NGO program cost
divided by number of beneficiaries); labor content as a percentage of the NGO program budget; number



- 50 -
of person-days of employment generated; and operating costs as a percentage of the NGO program
budget; (ii) obstacles and issues to be overcome and resolved; (iii) targeting; (iv) community participation;
(v) financial reporting; and, (vi) sustainability. Each of the NGO programs would also have a monitoring
schedule based on its specific characteristics (see paras. 28-30). In addition to this the SCF-US will
report to TASIF on achieved targets in the Shelter program in order to trigger release of payments by
TASIF to SCF-US. After receiving an Advance of 10% the second third ad fourth payments will be
released based on the following achieved outputs: second payment of 40% when three demonstration
houses have been completed and five hundred houses have been completed; third payment of 40% when
an additional 600 house have been completed; and the last payment of 10% when the remaining 900
houses have been completed
ASSUMPTIONS AND RISKS
32.   Major assumptions and risks include ensuring transparency and appropriate beneficiary selection,
ensuring the timely supply and monitoring the flow of a large volume of goods, ensuring quality and
performance of tile manufacture, and ensuring food quality and quantity. Assurance of food quality and
quantity available for FFW is required. In case of delays in delivery, or of food stocks approaching
their expiry date, SCF-US would negotiate an exchange of stocks with WFP or any other agency dealing
with commodity delivery in Tajikistan. Details of food quality control are given in Technical Note 3.
33.   Food For Work activities historically run the risk of distorting food market prices and depressing
local production. However, given the anticipated shortfall locally and nationally over the next two years,
this danger appears minimal. Regardless of such a forecast, SCF-US monitors local market prices of
food regularly, and will propose appropriate action should the FFW activities proposed here at any time
appear to be an inappropriate mechanism.



- 51 -
34.   The program assumes that the dissemination of tiles technology will be successful. This requires
not only the procurement of materials, but the training of local artisans in roof construction, tiling, quality
control and repair. This risk is justified by the need to depart from the use of asbestos sheet roofing, and
will be mitigated by continued close contact and collaboration between SCF and the contractor
throughout the duration of the project.
CONDITIONALITY
35.   The program funding is conditional to the stipulations set out and agreed upon in the legal
agreement between TASIF and SCF-US.
Technical Note 1: Material Requirement Calculation
Roofing Kit:
In order to complete 2,000 houses Save the Children-US will procure 100,000 pieces of planks, 60,000
pieces of cross beams, 2,900,000 pieces of roofing tiles, and 4,000 kg each of binding wire and nails.
Note:
Planks:      140mm*50mm*6000mm   =        0.042 CBM
Cross beams:   50mm*40mm*6000mm    =      0.012 CBM
Planks:      50 pcs. * 0.042       =      2.1 CBM
Cross Beams    30 pcs. * 0.012     =      0.36 CBM
TOTAL per house                    =      2.46 CBM
One standard house is 72 sq. m., with a roofing area estimated to be 100 sq. m. One house will receive
2.46 (for budgeting purpose rounded off to 2.5) cu m. of timber, 1500 tiles, 2 kg wire and 2 kg nails.

Technical Note 2: Food Requirement Calculation
36.    There will be labor compensation of the reconstruction work through food (Food for Work). In
order to complete the target of 2,000 houses, 180 MT of wheat flour, 180 MT of pinto beans and 60 MT
of vegetable oil will be required. SCF-US still has approximately 5,000 MT of USDA 1995 food
available, and is expecting an additional 6,000 MT under their USDA 1996 grant. The ration to be
distributed for Food for Work program beneficiaries will be wheat flour, pinto beans and vegetable oil.
It should be noted that the individual commodities may change, depending upon actual receipt from
USDA. The ration scale will be adjusted accordingly.
Food       Qt. Ihouse      Yr. 1           Yr. 2         Total Qt.       Total Value
(MT)     Qt.: 800 houses  Qt.: 1200 houses  Required (MT)  of Food ($)
Wheat Flour    0.09        72              108             180             97,200
Pinto Beans    0.0         72             108              180             93,600
Vegetable Oil    0         24             36               6           0  70,800
Total          0.21         168           252              420             261,600
Note: The value of the food commodities is calculated on average July 1996 market prices for the commodities: Wheat flour -
$ 0.54; Pinto beans - $ 0.52; and Vegetable oil - $1.18
The extent of destruction of houses ranges from 20% to 100%. Food calculation is based on an average
destruction rate of 75 % per house. Depending upon the quantity of different food commodity received,
necessary adjustments will made for the individual commodities to be distributed per house.
Technical Note 3: Food Quality Assurance
37.    The following steps are taken to ensure the food quality:
(a) On a semi-annual basis, commodity samples are taken by the doctor in the district hospital, who
will then analyze the food quality and certify whether the food is fit for human consumption..
(b) In the case of damaged bags, a similar testing is performed before the reconstitution is done.
(c) Warehousing facilities are fumigated with Bromistic methyl (45 gm/cum.) every six months.
After every fumigation, the fumigation company is responsible for performing the analysis of
commodity samples and providing a certificate concerning its fitness for human consumption.
It should be noted that food is generally not kept more than six months. This implies that there
will be only one fumigation required per tranche.
(d) In the event that food has been stored more than six months, SCF-US collaborates with other
humanitarian agencies who are in need of food for immediate distribution. SCF-US will loan the
needed quantity of commodities, and same quantity will be repaid to SCF-US once the other
agency's fresh stock comes.
(e) Food certified as unfit for human consumption is given to animal, fish or poultry farms.



- 54 -
ANNEX C
REPUBLIC OF TAJIKISTAN
PILOT POVERTY ALLEVIATION PROJECT
MICRO-CREDIT PROGRAM (SAVE THE CHILDREN-US)
SUMMARY
1.    The proposed program will focus on women in war-affected regions of Tajikistan to achieve in
two years the following specific objectives: (a) provide access to credit and mobilize savings for 3, 750
women in the Bokhtar, Vaksh, Vose, Kurgan-Tube and Kolkhozabad districts of Khatlon Region; (b)
generate additional income for 3750 people in the Bokhtar, Vaksh, Vose, Kurgan Tube and Kolkhozabad
districts of Khatlon Region; (c) positively impact approximately 18,750 children and other dependents
of the original 3750 borrowers; (d) develop the Group Guaranteed Loans and Savings (GGLS) program
into an independent local NGO or financial services institution that can operate independently within four
years a micro finance program that sustains access capital for poor women. The program is currently
operating in Kurgan-Tube, Bokhtar, Vaksh and Kolkhozabad districts of Western Khatlon. The proposed
expanded GGLS program will include Vose District. The existing program has been operating for over
one year. The expanded program will be for a period of two years and will begin in April 1997 and
continue until April 1999. It will be implemented by Save the Children-US.
OBJECTIVES
2.    Over 800,000 people were displaced from the war in 1992-93. The homes of more than 25,000
families were destroyed and those who fled lost all or most of their productive and household assets. The
800,000 displaced, particularly the 25,000 families who lost their homes, constitute the largest single
vulnerable group in Tajikistan today. In addition areas like Khulyab face extreme lack of economic
opportunities. Particularly poorer section of the community are facing extreme hardship for their
livelihood. It is crucial that they have the opportunity to reestablish their productivity.
3.    The social, political and economic upheavals of the last few years have precipitated the final
disintegration of an already dysfunctional institutional infrastructure. At present the Government of
Tajikistan does not have comprehensive policies for civic groups or rural banking institutions, restricting
the development of both.  The presence of arbitrary and obstructive tax policies further restrict
opportunities for people to create indigenous structures that would help protect and provide for
themselves. Government is not able to provide access or education to encourage alternative sources for
opportunities like small business development. Formal banks are not reliable and do not provide access
to credit for poor women. The lack of collateral among poor women, returnee refugees and internally
displaced people (IDPs) forces would-be entrepreneurs to enter high risk relationships for credit with
black market lenders. Poor women, returnee refugees and IDPs have no institutional structure other than
their equally depressed families and friends to assist on their livelihood. Most people receive little or no
cash for the work they perform for the collective farms and businesses. Alternative sources of income
and services are necessary for people to provide for their basic needs.



- 55 -
4.    The population now has a disproportionate gender and age composition. Many men were killed
in the war leaving behind women, children and the elderly. Historically in Tajikistan men have the
greater access to jobs for cash. However, the present circumstances create a great demand for capital
among women. Without access to credit and an active earning member in the family, life for the
remaining members has been very difficult. Even though women want to work to earn a living, they are
limited in their income generating activities due to a dearth of skills. The expanded program will provide
the essential capital for this otherwise alienated population through its proposed GGLS and provide
technical support so that women can improve and broaden their skills.
The specific objectives of the expanded program are the following:
(a) Sustainable reduction of poverty through the provision of access to credit and mobilization of
savings for 3,750 women in the Bokhtar, Vaksh, Vole, Kurgan-Tube and Kolkhozabad districts
of Khatlon Region.
(b) Generation of additional income for 3,750 people in the Bokhtar, Vaksh, Vose, Kurgan Tube and
Kolkhozabad Districts of Khatlon Region.
(c) Positive impact on approximately 18,750 children and other dependents of the original 3,750
borrowers.
(d) Development of the GGLS program into an independent local NGO or financial services
institution that can operate independently within four years a micro finance program that sustains
access capital for poor women.
(e) Development of common ground and bringing people together for their mutual benefit.
STRATEGY AND TARGETING
5.    The current strategy focuses on construction brigades and women's committees. Members in
brigades have the experience of working together which is considered to be a good way to initiate GGLS
groups. Initially, based on group interest and SCF-US's experience with a number of these brigades in
their present GGLS program, additional brigade members will be selected to participate in the GGLS
activities. If there is not an adequate number of women from the house building brigades in the same
localities to make a GGLS group, women who have not participated in the reconstruction may participate
in the savings and credit program. In order to be a member of the group the following criteria will be
used:
(a) Female (however, GGLS will experiment with a few male or mixed groups);
(b) From a poor family;
(c) Living permanently in the community;
(d) Only one member from a family is permitted and;



- 56 -
(e) Must possess some skills or experience relevant to proposed business activity.
6.     As a pilot test a few men's groups will also be formed and progress will be assessed based on
the achievement of the pilot test group expansion strategy will be determined. The number of groups in
the expanded program is shown below:

ACTIVITIES
7.     The average group size will be fifteen. All GGLS groups will organize themselves for a series
of meetings with SCF -US- staff to ensure that all the members of the groups are clear about their
responsibilities and expectations that they have of SCF (US). In these initial meetings the groups will
agree and commit to a written set of GGLS bylaws for the group including leadership selection. These
bylaws will include policies and procedures to ensure the long term sustainability and transparent
accountability of the groups loans funds.
8.     In order to build up capacity, the different training programs will be done at two different levels:
for national staff and for local institutions.
(a) Training sessions for national staff will focus on: GGLS theory; sustainability and how to achieve
it; how to present GGLS in the community; the role of the promoter; group formation and
bylaws; training in group dynamics; internal (group) savings and external accounts; first and
subsequent loan cycles; increasing loan amounts with each cycle; GGLS accounting, program and
financial reporting.
(b) For the local institutions, training will be given to those interested in leading an apex NGO. This
training will be based on a training needs assessment, and is likely to cover topics such as
leadership, management, accounting, recording keeping, credit management, market survey, and
new business creation. . The apex NGO will work as a micro credit institution and will give all
technical assistance required by the groups within five years of the start date of proposed
activities.
9.     The existing NGO capacity building project (which is a collaborative project of SCF-US and the
Counterpart Consortium) will assist project staff in designing different training programs. Through this
project, several of the SCF-US staff will be trained in Institutional Capacity Building. They, in turn, will
train other staff or members of micro-credit institutions. As needed, interested borrowers and members
of local NGOs will also be admitted to the training program conducted by the NGO Capacity Building



- 57 -
Training team. Training will be offered at the SCF-US's two training centers in Kurgan-Tube and
Dushanbe.
IMPLEMENTATION
10.   Accessibility to Credit and Savings. SCF-US will allocate to each group loan money from its
revolving fund budget. The group will approve its members' loans on the basis of the feasibility of the
economic activities proposed by the applicant, and the group's judgement of the applicant's ability to
implement the proposed activities and repay the loan. As there is no collateral in the group lending
program, therefore the group will guarantee repayment if the borrower in its group defaults. If a group
does not repay the loan when it is due that group's access to the loan money will be cut off or be
suspended until and unless due repayment is made. Groups that perform well will receive more loan
fund allocations from the GGLS program. As the group gains experience and performs well, the amount
of subsequent individual loans will increase as individuals develop their entrepreneurial capacity.
11.   Savings will be an important component of the program. Saving serves as an indicator of profit
and commitment. The GGLS program will require clients to save on a weekly basis, which will total
three percent of the loan in a cycle. However, program participants will be encouraged to save more.
Group savings perform many functions, including servicing as a guarantee for defaults and being available
for future capital expansion. Group participants will be encouraged to open individual or group saving
accounts once the banking facilities will be available in the working area.

12.   All 3,750 members will get loans in four three-month cycles. The loanee must repay the
previous cycle before getting the next. Upon the formation of the group, members will sign a contract
as a group with SCF -US or with the partner institution (when formed) about holding regular bi-weekly
group meetings, making regular repayments and sharing a commitment to help each other in the group
in order to fulfill program rules. Upon acquiring membership every member will be issued a membership
card. Loan disbursements as well as repayments will be done in Tajik Rubles. For each cycle a
repayment schedule of six bi-weekly installments will be followed. Until an apex NGO is formed, SCF-
US project staff will handle cash transactions and deposit individual group loan repayments in an SCF-US
safe in the SCF-US office. For depositing large amounts of cash, commercial banking facilities will be
used. In the existing pilot program the first cycle started with TR 1,200 ($20) which increased to TR
5,000 ($30) in the second, TR 12,000 ($40) in the third, and TR 15,000 ($55) in the fourth cycle. In



- 58 -
the case of urban groups TR 6,000, 12,000, 15,000, and 18,000 ($30, $40, $55, and $65 respectively)
were disbursed in 1-4 cycles respectively.
13.    At the close of the first year of the project 3,450 loans will be disbursed to 1,500 members.
Disbursed loans will be protected by 100 groups, averaging 15 members per group. In each of the first
and second quarters of the first year, 20 groups will enter the first loan cycle. In subsequent quarters
of the first year, 20 groups will enter the first loan cycle. In subsequent quarters of the first year, 30
groups will enter the first loan cycle. Similarly in the second year, 30 groups in each of the first and
second quarters and 45 groups each in the last two quarters, will enter the first cycle of the loan. Upon
the successful completion of a loan cycle, at the end of each quarter, groups will enter a new loan cycle
and will remain constant once they reach the fourth cycle. The Implementation Plan matrices below shows

installment.

MANAGEMENT
14.    The GGLS program will be supervised by a Program Advisor Manager (expatriate consultant).
He or she will be responsible for assisting with the design, implementation, supervision and overall
management of the GGLS program and will train the local staff to take over his or her duties after two
years. The program manager will be responsible for the GGLS unit office in Kurgan Tube and for
overall management of the program operations. The Program Manager will supervise the assistant
managers, who will be in charge of record keeping and financial management. They will be responsible
for the satellite offices in Kurgan Tube and Vose, and for the program operations through these offices.
A Monitoring and Training Officer will be responsible for the design and installation of the monitoring
system, and for training the staff of partner institutions to monitor the credit program. Group promoters
(who are the front-line of the GGLS program) are in charge of group formation, disbursing and collection
of loans, and savings mobilization, and they are supervised by unit supervisors, who are responsible for
record keeping in individual passbooks and for supervising the collection and disbursement activities.
The groups are organized with 15 to 20 members each, with a chairperson, a vice chairperson and a
secretary. Support staff (accountants, secretary, and drivers) will assist with the regular functioning of
the credit operation and management of the institution. In addition, GGLS staff will have the benefit of
support from the SCF/US Field Office Director, Assistant Director, and Assistant Field Office Director
for Finance and Administration.



- 60 -
ENVIRONMENTAL ASPECTS
15.   The project will have no adverse impacts on the environment. The maximum size of the loan is
100 and ill be used mainly for trading activities.
SUSTAINABILITY
16.   The Micro Credit Group Guarantee Loans and Savings program aims to develop the existing
program into a sustainable local NGO or financial services institution that can operate independently
within four years and can sustain the access to capital for poor women. Within two years from the start
date of this upscaled project a core group of borrowers and GGLS staff will begin participating in SC
sponsored training and will produce an agenda for the development of a local apex institution that can
fully operate a micro credit program within four years. Various operating responsibilities related to the
GGLS program will be handed over to the clients groups through the four year period as their capacity
dictates. In the fourth year of this program groups receiving credit and savings services will be managed
and monitored by this apex NGO. Furthermore, in the fourth year of this program the apex NGO will
be provided a one time grant to run the credit and savings program and establish links to other
organizations so that its members can access more resources. The GGLS program will also be linked,
through the monitoring and evaluation process, to the TASIF according to the operational manual
guidelines. This will enable a strengthening of institutional replicability and sustainability. To address
the issue of financial sustainability, savings will be an important component of the project. Savings
serves as an indicator of profit and commitment as well as sustainability. The GGLS program will
require clients to save on a weekly basis which will total 3% of the loan in a cycle. Group savings
perform many functions, including serving as a guarantee for defaults and being available for future
capital expansion. Group members will be encouraged to open individual or group savings accounts once
the banking facilities are available in the area. All 3750 group members will get loans in 3 month cycles.
At the close of the first year of the project 3450 loans will be disbursed to 1500 members. Disbursed
loans will be protected by 100 groups, averaging 15 members per group. In the first and second quarter
of the first year 20 groups each will enter the first loan cycle. Similarly in the second year 30 groups
each will enter the first loan cycle. Similarly, in the second year 30 groups each in first and second
quarters and 45 groups each in last two quarters will join the first cycle of loan. Upon the successful
completion of a loan cycle in the end of each quarter groups will enter a new loan cycle and will remain
constant once they reach the fourth cycle. Repayments of loan with interest from the first year will be
revolved for continued funding in subsequent years. the proposed loan funding will allow all groups to
reach the forth loan cycle and continue receiving loans indefinitely provided additional funding is secured
to pay portion of the program costs. SCF estimates the program will be 6 % operationally self sustainable
a the end of the first year and 27%, 72% and 144% at the end of the second, third and fourth years
respectively with standard interest charge of 10% to which 3% will be added to make the total service
charge 13%. However the 3% will be deposited in groups names and will remain as the savings for the
group. Groups will have full authority in the utilization of the savings fund.



- 61 -
LINKS WITH THE TASIF
17.   The GGLS program will, as stipulated in the TASIF operational manual guidelines be monitored
and evaluated by the TASIF through an integrated monitoring and evaluation for the entire PPAP program
(see Annex L). The GGLS program will provide TASIF with regular monthly, quarterly, six monthly
and annual reports.

MONITORING AND REPORTING
18. Bi weekly Monitoring will include:
(a) Group information: Group namne; group cycle; meeting number; treasurer's name and signature;
number of children and other family members reported by group; names of group members;
number of children and other family members reported by each group member; business activity
of each group member; promoters'name and signature; date prepared and submitted; reviewed
and signed by promoter's supervisor; date reviewed and signed by promoter's supervisor;
(b) Attendance:  Number of members in the group this cycle; number of members attending this
meeting; percentage of attendance this meeting;
(c) Funds coming in this meeting: Total principal collected this meeting; total interest collected this
meeting;
(d) Funds going out this meeting: Loans disbursed this meeting;
(e) Amounts to date: Amount overdue; amount outstanding; amount collected by promoter;
(f) Group Cycle Monitoring will include: Promoters name; signature; date prepared and submitted;
reviewed and signed by promoters supervisor; date reviewed and signed by promoter's
supervisor;
(g) Attendance: Number of projected attenders if 100% attendance per month and cycle; number of
attenders per month and cycle; group % of attendance per month and cycle;
(h) Portfolio Statistics: Number of loans disbursed to group members this cycle; amount disbursed
to group members this cycle; average size of disbursement this cycle; average size of
disbursement this cycle; amount projected to be collected per bi weekly meeting; amount
projected to be collected per cycle; amount collected per biweekly meeting; amount collected this
cycle; amount over due this cycle;
(i) Overall Self Sufficiency: Overall operating expenses interest and fees collected each biweekly
meeting; interest and fees collected each cycle; self sustainability % for each cycle (amount
collected divided by the sum of operating expenses, inflation and loss due to defaulters);
(j) Evaluation of group and inter-group behavior: Group ability to organize and prepare for
initiation of loans and manage loans for income'generating activities; the quality and quantity of
contact of the group and its members to other groups; the ability of women's committees or other
loan partners to support the groups and facilitate inter group contact; the number of participants
involved in productive activities in the groups; the impact of the project on the health, food
security and development of children of the participants.

Client assessments (will be based on a survey of a sample group of clients, composed of one client

groups. A total of 40 clients to be surveyed.
At the one year evaluation the survey would be of the 40 clients and in addition 60 from each one

How much revenue is earned from the loan?

this happened?

repay)?
How much savings?
Has client withdrawn savings? If yes, then for what use?
INTEGRATED EVALUATION
20.   An independent integrated evaluation at mid-term and project completion would be contracted by
TASIF to be undertaken by a consultant leading a multi-disciplinary team of local and international
experts. Implementing INGOs including SCF-US would each nominate a team member, but the team as
a whole would evaluate all aspects of both components of the project including the SCF-US GGLS
program. The team would visit project sites, review reports and indicator measurements in the light of
its own observations and investigations, and seek to establish lessons to be learned both at mid-term and
for Project follow-up. It would report both to TASIF and to the Bank. Further details are in Annex L.
21.   Key objective indicators will be measured and monitored by the Project in order to track, in a
consistent and comparable fashion, the essentials of performance against objectives. The key objective
indicators are summarized in Table 1 of Annex L. The first Project objective of impact on the incomes
of the poor will be measured across all sub-components by five key indicators, of which two will indicate
the extent to which this objective has been met in a cost effective and participatory fashion. In addition,
each sub-component will be evaluated according to three specific indicators relating to the particular
program. For the GGLS program these three specific indicators are: active portfolio; household incomes;
and productive enterprises. These indicators measure impact, coverage and sustainability. The second
Project objective of capability building would be measured by two general indicators across all
components, with a third relating specifically to the training of TASIF staff. Thus each sub-component
will be evaluated on ten indicators, of which seven are common to all sub-components of the Project, and
three are specific to each sub-component.  These indicators, and the specific measures and instruments



- 65 -
to compile them, would be refined in a pilot baseline exercise.
22. Monitoring and evaluation activities have already been a prominent feature of the existing GGLS
program, and both the findings and systems used will be built upon in the scaled-up program. The Project
would closely monitor the scaled-up program. Specifically, SCF-US would be asked to report to the
TASIF on a quarterly basis on the following: (i) summary of achievements: number of beneficiaries
reached (direct and indirect) versus goal; cost per beneficiary (total NGO program cost divided by
number of beneficiaries); labor content as a percentage of the NGO program budget; number of person-
days of employment generated; and operating costs as a percentage of the NGO program budget; (ii)
obstacles and issues to be overcome and resolved; (iii) targeting; (iv) community participation; (v)
financial reporting; and, (vi) sustainability. Each of the NGO programs would also have a monitoring
schedule based on its specific characteristics (see paras. 18-19). In addition to this the SCF-US will
report to TASIF on achieved targets in the GGLS program in order to trigger release of payments by
TASIF to SCF-US (see para 25).
ASSUMPTIONS AND RISKS
23.   Assumptions are that the security situation will remain stable and that the economic crisis will
not worsen which will allow clients to continue to repay and borrow loans on time and steadily add to
their savings accounts. The rate of inflation it is assumed will continue to decrease or stabilize. Along
with the risk of defaults due to a deterioration in the social and economic conditions, there is the existing
risk of harassment of GGLS's clients by militia who charge exorbitant rates of illegal fees from market
vendors and often confiscate their goods. Added to this risk of theft from clients is the risk of theft from
GGLS staff or from the GGLS office of loans and savings funds. Banking facilities in the program area
are non-existent therefore cash amounts need to be hand carried to and from Dushanbe to Kurgan Tube
and then to and from clients to GGLS offices.
24.   The program funding is conditional to the stipulations set out and agreed upon in the legal
agreement between TASIF and SCF (US). Key steps which need to be taken prior to contract signing
have been agreed upon with SCF US for the GGLS program. Of those listed below key steps such as
introduction of savings concept to all existing groups, the implementation of savings with existing groups
and the revision of the application form to include socio economic baseline data on clients has been
initiated as of September 1996. The conditions for signing the contract are as follows:
(i)  Initiation of recruitment process of Project Advisor, Project Manager, Assistant Project
Manager, eight group promoters, two supervisors and one monitoring and training officer by
contract signing date.
(ii) Revision of Application form to include key social-economic baseline information on group
members.
(iii) Introduction of savings concept to existing groups and Implementing savings with e x i s t i n g
groups.



- 66 -

ANNEX D
REPUBLIC OF TAJIKISTAN
PILOT POVERTY ALLEVIATION PROJECT
PROGRAM OF SUPPORT TO FEMALE-HEADED HOUSEHOLDS
(SAVE THE CHILDREN UK)
SUMMARY
1.    The proposed program will focus on female-headed households in Western Tajikistan (Vaksh,
Aini, Panjkent, Dushanbe and Shaartuz Districts). It will assist 3,700 of the most vulnerable households,
and build the capacity of up to 16 local organizations by providing practical experience in effective
approaches to poverty alleviation. The program will have two components: (1) Support to Vulnerable
Households in Khatlon Province, which will reach 2,100 female-headed households with agricultural
inputs and social services; and (2) Capacity Building for Poverty Alleviation, which will strengthen 16
local government and non governmental organizations and reach up to 1,600 female-headed households
with appropriate community-centered poverty alleviation activities.
OBJECTIVES
2.    Levels of household poverty are high and increasing through out Tajikistan, and the most
vulnerable groups are suffering the direct consequences in ill health, poor nutrition, inadequate housing
and lost opportunities for education. Household incomes have fallen as a result of dramatically reduced
opportunities for employment and access to resources for self employment is very limited. Compounding
the problem of household poverty is the collapse of the basic health and education services, due to the
central government's inability to finance them adequately. Access to health care for the poorest has been
severely curtailed by the rising cost of treatment as well as supply shortages. Local response to this
situation is hampered by the lack of experience and capacity within both local government and NGOs to
understand and respond to the needs of vulnerable households. This is due in part to a inadequate
knowledge and skills concerning practical approaches to poverty alleviation among staff of local
government and NGOs.
3.    In Khatlon Province, the Districts of Vaksh and Bokhtar, where SCF (UK) has been working
since 1994, were among those most seriously affected by the civil war. There are a large number of
vulnerable households - many of which are female headed and thus face considerable social as well as
economic obstacles. SCF's experience in the area indicates the importance of addressing all these issues,
including in particular the decline in educational and child care services, and the need for access to other
social services.
4.    The objectives of the project are ensure that a total of 3,700 most vulnerable households gain
access agriculture assets and social services such as day-care centers and schools, and to build local
capacity of 16 local organizations to deliver such services. The project consists of two main components:



- 68 -
Component 1: Support to Vulnerable Households in Khatlon Province
5.    This component builds on the experience of SCF (UK) in working with female-headed households
in Bokhtar/Vaksh districts, and has the following objectives:
*  to increase levels of household income for 2,100 vulnerable households by July 1999,
through the provision of agricultural assets such as livestock, seeds and tools as well as
technical advice;
*  to strengthen local capacity to support day-care centers and schools serving 30 villages, by
establishing community based mechanisms for the management of grants and revolving funds
generated by agriculture inputs;
*  to promote access to available services and resources through the dissemination of
information and advice, group formation, and legal support to vulnerable h households
indirectly to the population of 30 villages.
Component 2: Capacity Building for Poverty Alleviation
6.    In order to start extending poverty alleviation activities beyond Khatlon province, and to test the
applicability of project approaches in other circumstances the aim of this component is:
C to enable up to 1600 vulnerable households in four areas of the country to achieve higher
levels of self reliance and greater access to income support;
* to improve the capacity of selected local organizations (government and non-government) to
identify and support appropriate community centered poverty alleviation activities.
STRATEGY AND TRAINING
7.    The program will provide productive assets to individual female-headed households, in order to
improve household food security and, where possible, generate a family income through the sale of
produce. SCF's experience indicates that this approach can be successful, provided that support
appropriate and based upon adequate consultation with beneficiaries about the scale of assistance and the
nature of assets to be provided. The introduction of a revolving fund element makes such participation
even more important.
8.    It has also become apparent that to achieve sustained impact across the full range of problems
faced by vulnerable households, poverty alleviation activities must also address social and other non-
economic obstacles, and that this requires support to organization at the community level over a period
of years rather then months. Community level organization is the major challenge in introducing this
approach in other areas.
9.    While continuing to ensure the appropriateness of material inputs distributed to individual
households, SCF (UK) will seek ways to improve the overall quality of the productive assets provided.
In rural areas this will include research into the feasibility of introducing improved wheat and potato seed



- 69 -
(particularly imported varieties). Different approaches will be developed in urban districts covered under
the local capacity building component.
10.   Field staff will play a critical role in all project activities, through informing vulnerable families
about their rights as well as about existing services and assistance programs. For example, experience
has shown that explaining the land reform process and the mechanisms for applying for land, as well as
following up on households requests for land with local and national authorities, can result in significant
benefits.
ACTIVITIES
Component 1: Support to Vulnerable households in Khatlon Province:
11.   In Vaksh District, SCF(UK) will identify 30 villages with a high proportion of vulnerable
(particularly FHH) households. These villages will be located around local schools and day-care centers.
Based on the level of productive assets and social conditions of each household SCF (UK) will classify
vulnerable families according to criteria which have been developed for the ongoing project.
12.   By the end of July 1997, two lists of FHHs will be identified by SCF field workers. The initial
700 FHH will form groups of 20 to 30 households. Each household will receive productive assets
according to their level of need up to a maximum equivalent to $150 per household. All households will
receive inputs on condition that they undertake to repay the equivalent value in cash or kind. The
repayment rate will be set at between 100 and 120 percent over a period to be determined by the SCF
(UK) depending on local conditions.
13.   Most of the funds repaid will be used to provide similar assistance to the second list of 700
FHHs, and as these receive their material inputs, a third group of up to 700 vulnerable households (some
of which may not be female headed) will be identified to receive assistance once further funds are
available.
14.   Any balance of repaid funds will be provided to local schools or day-care centers. These funds
will be managed by a committee including representatives of vulnerable households, other parents, pupils,
teachers and local authorities. The committees will be supported by an SCF (UK) field worker.
15.   Support to schools and day-care centers will also include one-off grants (possibly in the form of
food for work for staff) as well as assistance in the formation of community management boards and in
the continuing management of income from he local revolving funds.
16.   An integral part of all this work will be the dissemination of information. Field workers will
promote group formation, provide advice and legal support, and help to mediate with local authorities
and service providers.
Component 2: Capacity building for Poverty Alleviation
17.   This component will develop practical skills among staff of local government and non-government
organizations. It is expected that these organizations will work at the community level to assist vulnerable
families in the identification of pilot projects which focus on income generation and improved access to
essential services.



- 70 -
18.   Potential local partners will be identified from among local government agencies and NGOs in
Aini, Panjkent, Dushanbe and Shaartuz to implement small scale poverty alleviation projects targeted at
vulnerable households. The role of these partners organizations will be critical to the success of this
component and will require close monitoring and support by SCF (UK) staff to ensure that projects are
appropriate and targeted to vulnerable households.
19.   Training will be provided for potential partners in the above-mentioned regions in participatory
approaches to needs assessment, project appraisals and design, implementation and monitoring.
Perspective partners will then be invited to submit proposals for small-scale poverty alleviation projects
targeted at approximately 100 vulnerable households. Targeting criteria as well as levels and forms of
material assistance will be based on SCF experience in Khatlon, but may vary slightly depending on local
circumstances.
20.   On the basis of proposals received, SCF (UK) will select up to 16 small-scale pilot projects
(approximately four in each of the four regions), for implementation over a two-year period.
Disbursement of funds and monitoring of implementation will be accompanied by further training and
exchanges of experience among partner organizations and SCF as well as other interested agencies.
IMPLEMENTATION
21.   See attached implementation plan.
MANAGEMENT
22.   Overall management of the project will be the responsibility of SCF (UK)'s program manger for
Tajikistan based in Dushanbe. Day to day operations will be managed by a project manager based in
Khatlon with support form the program administrator for procurement and logistics as well as financial
monitoring, and from the training coordinator for local capacity building.
23.   In addition to national and international staff SCF (UK) will draw on the services of advisors and
consultants for specific areas of work.
24.   SCF (UK) activities in Tajikistan from part of its Central Asia program managed by the program
director based in Tashkent. Support to the Central Asia program is provided by the South Asia desk in
SCF London and by the Kathmandu advisory office.
ENVIRONMENTAL ASPECTS
25.   Major environmental concerns in Khatlon province include the impact of intensive cotton
cultivation, management of solid wastes, growing environmental health problems, and deforestation.
26.   Environmental considerations will be incorporated into the assessment and monitoring of both
components of the program.



- 71 -
SUSTAINABILITY
27.   Sustainability may be considered at three levels:  the household, community and service
institution. The program aims to deliver rapid support to vulnerable households, as well as to build the
capacity of local institutions to provide such support in the future. At the household level, the
implementation of this pilot will assist in establishing the criteria for sustainability, including: the levels
of support necessary to make a significant impact on welfare; the level and rate of repayments which
households can maintain; the levels of technical support and training required to make full use of material
inputs; and the implications of technical inputs for household resource (in particular labor) allocation.
28.   At community level, intensive training of group leaders and management committee members will
be undertaken in order to ensure the cohesion of FHH groups and school/day-care management structures,
and their continued role in supporting vulnerable households.
29.   At the institutional level, the project will work closely with TASIF ad individual ministries and
other participating agencies in order to learn jointly the lessons of implementation and contribute to
national capacity.
LINKS WITH THE TASIF
30.   This is the key element in term of institutional sustainability for poverty alleviation. The
experience gained by this program will provide lessons applicable to TASIF. In order for these lessons
to be shared between SCF (UK) and TASIF a close working relationship will be established during
project preparation which will require the creation of informal and formal fora for collaboration at
national level. The monitoring and reporting mechanisms will be a major means of achieving this
objective.
BUDGET
31. The total budget is $1,031,744 for the Program: $423,497in Year 1, and $447684in Year 2 and
$160,562.5 in Year 3.
MONITORING AND REPORTING
32.   SCF(UK) will maintain an internal monitoring system for key program indicators (table 0).
Quarterly narrative and financial reports will be submitted to GOT within thirty days of the end of each
quarter. A final report will be submitted by SCF(UK) to GOT within sixty days of the end of the project
period. Reports will be prepared by the Program Manager, Tajikistan.
