INTEGRATED EVALUATION
33.    An independent integrated evaluation at mid-term and project completion would be contracted by
TASIF to be undertaken by a consultant leading a multi-disciplinary team of local and international
experts. Implementing INGOs including SCF-UK would each nominate a team member, but the team
as a whole would evaluate all aspects of both components of the project including the SCF-UK program
of support to female headed households. The team would visit project sites, review reports and indicator
measurements in the light of its own observations and investigations, and seek to establish lessons to be
learned both at mid-term and for Project follow-up. It would report both to TASIF and to the Bank.
Further details are in Annex L.



- 73 -
34.    Key objective indicators will be measured and monitored by the Project in order to track, in a
consistent and comparable fashion, the essentials of performance against objectives. The key objective
indicators are summarized in Table 1 of Annex L. The first Project objective of impact on the incomes
of the poor will be measured across all sub-components by five key indicators, of which two will indicate
the extent to which this objective has been met in a cost effective and participatory fashion. In addition,
each sub-component will be evaluated according to three specific indicators relating to the particular
program. For the SCF-UK support to female headed households program these three specific indicators
are: number of participating FHHs; household incomes; and household food productivity. These
indicators measure impact, coverage and sustainability. The second Project objective of capability
building would be measured by two general indicators across all components, with a third relating
specifically to the training of TASIF staff.    Thus each sub-component will be evaluated on ten
indicators, of which seven are common to all sub-components of the Project, and three are specific to
each sub-component.   These indicators, and the specific measures and instruments to compile them,
would be refined in a pilot baseline exercise.
35.    Monitoring and evaluation activities are already a feature of the existing support to female headed
households program, and both the findings and systems used will be built upon in the scaled-up program.
The Project would closely monitor the scaled-up program. Specifically, SCF-UK would be asked to
report to the TASIF on a quarterly basis on the following: (i) summary of achievements: number of
beneficiaries reached (direct and indirect) versus goal; cost per beneficiary (total NGO program cost
divided by number of beneficiaries); labor content as a percentage of the NGO program budget; number
of person-days of employment generated; and operating costs as a percentage of the NGO program
budget; (ii) obstacles and issues to be overcome and resolved; (iii) targeting; (iv) community participation;
(v) financial reporting; and, (vi) sustainability. Each of the NGO programs would also have a monitoring
schedule based on its specific characteristics (see para. 32). In addition to this the SCF-UK will report
to TASIF on achieved targets in the support to female headed households program in order to trigger
release of payments by TASIF to SCF-US (see para 39).
ASSUMPTIONS AND RISKS
36.    The greatest risk to the Program is the uncertain security situation which persists in some project
areas. To address this risk, the implementation plans will remain flexible to respond to any changes in
the local situation.
37.    The second risk relates to the limited capacity of local organizations to implement program
activities. Activities within the program itself are directed at increasing this capacity. In addition, to
address this risk the capacity of potential partner organizations will be assessed prior to implementation;
the number of such organizations will be limited; outreach activities will be limited to one hundred
households per organization.
A number of more specific risks could also affect the project:
(a) attempts or influence national staff recruitment: SCF (UK) has a policy of advertising all
posts, with standard application forms and interview records;



- 74 -
(b) attempts to influence beneficiary selection: SCF(UK) has established and published criteria
for beneficiary selection;
(c) fluctuation in prices of agricultural inputs: SCF(UK) monitors market prices and, where
possible, ensures that purchases are made at the optimum time;
(d) harassment of national staff and beneficiaries during distribution; SCF(UK) approaches local
authorities in the project area before beginning project activities to request support and ensure
security;
(e) theft from warehouse or beneficiaries' homes or land; SCF(UK) ensures that adequate
security for warehouse facilities is provided and works with local authorities to resolve cases
of theft;
(f) disease of potatoes, wheat, vegetables and livestock: SCF(UK) will consult closely with
agricultural and livestock specialists and ensure that training and support are provided to
beneficiaries;
(g) attempts to influence school/day-care committees; SCF(UK) will monitor committees closely
and may withdraw support in cases where funds are misused.
38.   Payments by TASIF to SCF (UK) will be made in four tranches of 10%, 40%, 40% and 10%.
The first payment will be an advance payment of 10% of the total budget amount and will be made by
TASIF to SCF (UK) upon Credit effectiveness. Subsequent payments will be made to SCF (UK) by
TASIF based on the condition that achievable outputs agreed to in the legal agreement (appendix B) are
met. These are as follows:
Second Payment of 40% when:
700 vulnerable FHH identified
700 vulnerable households categorized
Group leaders appointed
Agriculture packages as defined by groups with procurement plans
Wheat seed purchased and available for distribution

ANNEX E
REPUBLIC OF TAJIKISTAN
PILOT POVERTY ALLEVIATION PROJECT
AGRICULTURAL REFORM PROGRAM (AGA KHAN FOUNDATION)
SUMMARY
1.    The Agricultural Reform Program  (ARP) is being implemented by the Pamir Relief and
Development Program (PRDP) in collaboration with the Aga Khan Foundation (AKF) in the Gorno-
Badakhshan Autonomous Oblast (GBAO), the mountainous region of eastern Tajikistan. Since its
inception in 1993, the program has grown more rapidly than anticipated, and PRDP has specific plans
for its significant expansion in the next few years in the areas irrigation and new land development. In
1997/1998 ARP will complete the construction of 32 gravity-flow irrigation channels (107 kilometers)
in six of GBAO's seven districts'' which will enable the cultivation of 3,877 hectares of new privately
managed land. Some 1,600 small resource farmers will participate. PRDP will provide technical advice
along with provision of improved seed, fertilizer, and other inputs on credit. In addition, the program
will furnish resources for PRDP's expansion through the development of district offices of the NGO.
OBJECTIVES
2.     Due to geo-political reasons, the region of GBAO has always been isolated from much of the rest
of Tajikistan. In spite of its isolation, the Soviets did establish an extensive system of collective (kolkhoz)
and state (sovkhoz) farms with centralized management. With little arable land and misguided decisions
of central planners, however, GBAO was heavily dependent upon imports from other regions of the
Soviet Union, and 80% of needed foodstuffs was brought in annually. After the collapse of the Soviet
Union in 1991, the residents of GBAO faced severe hardship and a widespread system of humanitarian
food distribution was established by the Aga Khan Foundation (AKF). This continues today. The major
needs of the populace are the provision of food, along with the creation of employment opportunities.
Some 80% of the labor force is estimated to be unemployed. It is in this context that the Agricultural
Reform Program is especially relevant.
3.    By charter, AKF is a development institution and not a relief organization. Thus in 1992 it
established the first local NGO in GBAO, the PRDP (Pamir Relief and Development Program), which
has been active in increasing the production and productivity of the region's agricultural sector through
the ARP, along with continuing its humanitarian assistance.
1/ These districts include Darwaz, Vanch, Rushan, Shugnan, Roshtkala, and Ishkashim; only the district of Murghab is
excluded because its populace is largely pastoral.



- 77 -
4.    The long-term goal of the ARP is to establish self-sufficiency in and reliance upon staple food
crops in GBAO through the fostering of increased efficiency and intensification of land use in an
environmentally sound way throughout the region. The basic objectives involved in the expansion of
ARP in GBAO for the coming years of 1997-1998 are as follows:
1. To expand the area under food crop production;
2. To increase food crop production and productivity;
3. To establish and support private farm management;
4. To develop human and institutional capacity in agriculture.
5.    The major targets to be achieved in the coming years include the following:
1997    1998    TOTAL
# channel systems
completed (within year)  19   13      32
# kilometers of channels
completed (within year)  31.8 km  76.0 km  107.8 km
# hectares of land brought
under cultivation
(within year)      325 ha   627 ha  952 ha
# hectares of land to be
brought under culti-
vation by 2004                    3,877 ha
6.    Focus will be upon small resource farmers throughout GBAO. Assuming that 1,600 farmers are
included in the program by 1998, and with an average family size of 8 persons, a total of 12,800
individuals will directly benefit from this expansion of ARP. The improvement in crop production and
the corresponding development of markets will in turn help all consumers of agricultural products in the
participating rural communities and in the region in general.
STRATEGY AND TARGETING
7.    PRDP works only with farmers who want to become (or have become) private farmers. Private
ownership of land is not important, but private management is. PRDP motivates farmers to negotiate
with the Government of GBAO for land (both previously cultivated and new plots) and assists them in
negotiating with kolkhoz authorities and district officials in order to obtain formal written contracts for



- 78 -
private management which define and guarantee land access rights.z' Subsequently PRDP helps private
farmers by providing agricultural inputs (improved seed, fertilizer, and fuel) on credit, along with
technical advice and assistance in cropping and irrigation.
8.      Since ARP began, villagers' first and foremost concern has been the need to bring additional
lands under cultivation in GBAO.3' Thus the NGO decided to assist farmers in channel construction and
new land development. The specific strategy is as follows:
*   Villagers desiring PRDP assistance in channel construction and new land development must
first come together, agree to work on the channel, and discuss their plans.
*   The farmers form a group and elect a sardar (leader) who will be the primary negotiator with
both government and PRDP.
*   The group prepares a formal written request to the government. The sardar then negotiates
with the government officials and obtains permission in writing for the farmers in his group
to utilize the designated unused lands for private management once the channel is completed.
*   The PRDP Agronomy Supervisor must then visit the proposed new land and ascertain that
it has suitable potential for cultivation. The PRDP Irrigation Engineer must also visit the
land, ascertain that the channel construction is feasible, and calculate its cost.
*   PRDP finally signs a formal agreement with the farmers' group stating that it will help with
channel construction and land development (i.e. technical assistance and materials).
*   Villagers agree to provide all manual labor required for channel construction, which is
fostered by Food-For-Work financed by GTZ.
9.      PRDP does not target the poor per se. At present individual village households and communities
are quite homogenous in their needs and, as noted above, all of the residents of GBAO face severe
hardships. If, with time, communities and/or households become more stratified economically, PRDP
will re-examine its policy.
2/ The specific PRDP-related agricultural reform processes in the area of farm restructuring are consistent with the National
Government's land reform and farm restructuring program that is currently being implemented on a pilot basis in other parts
of the country and is supported by IDA's Agricultural Recovery and Social Production Credit .
3/ At present a total of approximately 13,000 hectares of land in GBAO is under cultivation, which include both kolkhoz
plots and PRDP-assisted privately managed lands. Grain yields from these fields fall far short of the necessary 30,000
metric tons of wheat annually required for GBAO, however, and rations continue to be distributed to all of the populace
with AKF assistance. An estimated 10,000 hectares of potential land for cultivation exists throughout the region which
presently lack irrigation sources. Although of varying quality, some 4,000-6,000 hectares exhibit very good potential for
wheat, barley, alfalfa, and potatoes.



- 79 -
ACTIVITIES
9.     The specific activities included here pertain to the four major objectives of ARP:
1. To expand the area under food crop production:
Activities:
a. increase the amount of land under food crop production;
b. establish new irrigation systems;
c. establish an intensive cropping system;
d. use Food-For-Work for irrigation channel construction and new land development.
2. To increase food crop production and productivity:
Activities:
a. supply agricultural inputs (improved seed, fertilizer, and fuel) on credit;
b. foster high-quality seed multiplication and seed distribution between farmers.
3. To establish and support private farm management:
Activities:
a. upgrade competence among farmers in negotiating with kolkhoz directors;
b. establish private farm management;
c. assist in establishing an open market and encourage sale of surplus produce.
4. To develop human and institutional capacity in agriculture:
Activities:
a. train PRDP staff to help private farmers negotiate agreements with the Government for
private use of land, conduct irrigation activities; provide    assistance in land
development;
b. train farmers in resource (water) and crop management.
IMPLEMENTATION
10.    By 1996 some 32 agreements had been signed by PRDP with the same number of farmers' groups
throughout GBAO; these range in size from 10-100 members.4' TABLE 1 contains a list of these groups
by district, which usually take the names of their respective village communities.
4/ Not all applications received by PRDP have been approved; a number of channels were found to be too expensive to
construct.



- 80 -
11.    One irrigation channel is being constructed in each scheme.  These small-scale gravity-flow
channels vary greatly in length, with an average of approximately 7 kilometers. The sardar (elected
leader) organizes his fellow villagers to provide the manual labor and coordinates the work. The specific
hectarage to be cultivated when the channels are completed also varies greatly, with some supporting 5-10
individual plots of privately managed land while others servicing 70 or more.5'
12.    Since Spring 1996 a GTZ-funded Food-For-Work component has been established in ARP for
farmers participating in channel construction, and this has proved very successful.t' Individuals are
reimbursed with wheat flour according to the amount of work they complete. The PRDP Irrigation
Engineer initially calculates the number of person-days form the channel construction, with one day
entitling the worker to 500 grams of flour. After every 500 meters the work is evaluated and, if of
quality, Food-For-Work is
distributed.
13.    TABLE 1 contains detailed information about the ARP channel construction schedule in 1997-
1998. During 1997 construction will be undertaken in all 32 sites, and by the end of the year 19 channels
will be completed. During 1998 construction will continue in the remaining 13 sites.
14.    In each site the PRDP Engineer will supply necessary cement, tools, etc., to the local sardar.
The amount of these materials specific to the planned channel has previously been determined by PRDP
staff and is recorded by both PRDP and the sardar. During construction the sardar will coordinate the
manual labor, check how many people work on the channel each day, record individuals' names, and also
keep careful accounts of how much material is utilized. Materials are either stored at the sardar's house
or in another local storehouse. village groups often collect contributions from members in kind (wheat,
etc.) to pay skilled labor to run the tractors, compressors, etc., involved in construction.
15.    One PRDP staff member Engineering/Agricultural specialist) in each district will be responsible
for coordinating all of this work, and he will monitor each channel construction. Quarterly reports will
be filed with PRDP Headquarters concerning all channels in his respective district. Each 500 meters the
sardar will also take stock of who has worked on the channel and distribute Food-For-Work accordingly
(500 grams wheat flour per day of work).
16.    TABLE 2 presents PRDP's Land Development Activity Plan and pertains to the new plots to be
brought under cultivation following the completion of irrigation channels. A longer channel does not
necessarily mean that more land will be able to be cultivated; each system is specific to its own terrain
and land quality. A significant portion (952 hectares)Z' of the newly irrigated land will come into
production by the close of 1998. However, as TABLE 3 also illustrates, some plots of new land will not
be fully developed until 2004. A number of factors are involved here: length of channel and time
necessary to complete, time necessary to level and develop land, quality/fertility of the land itself, etc.
51 There is no direct relationship between length of channel and hectarage able to be irrigated. A comparatively short
channel may irrigate many plots of land, while a long channel may irrigate only a few.
6/ Reimbursement for work such as this is an important change, albeit small, from the extensive food ration system now in
place throughout the Pamirs.
7/ To date, some 109.5 kilometers of channels have already been completed, with a total of 215 hectares of new land having
been cultivated. These figures are also reflected in TABLE 2.

17.   PRDP will assist the farmers in the development of the new land which includes help and
guidance in leveling the land and construction of offtake channels from the main channel. The PRDP
Irrigation Engineer will also conduct assessments on water use for each system. The PRDP Agriculture
Supervisor and district office Agriculturalists will provide on-going ARP inputs: advice on what to grow,
land development, provision of improved seeds, fertilizer, and other inputs on credit (to be paid back in
cash or kind following harvest).
18.   Through discussions with participating farmers and analysis of land intended for irrigation, the
following distribution of crops is anticipated on the new plots:
70% wheat
10% barley
10% alfalfa
10% potatoes/vegetables
In general, the larger plots will be planted in grains, and the smaller in vegetables. Wheat will largely
be consumed by the producing household, while the other crops will be marketed.
19.   Planning workshops of one week in length for all PRDP staff involved in ARP will be held prior
to each cropping season in April and November. At this time progress to date will be reviewed,
techniques of negotiation with government and farmer will be improved, and new crops, seeds, fertilizers,
and other technical innovations to be included in the program will be discussed in detail. The status of
ARP will be reviewed district-by-district, with staff planning the coordination of land development and
other agricultural inputs for the newly irrigated plots.
20.    Staff will make frequent contact with the communities involved in ARP in their respective districts
and will continue to support the farmers to become active decision-makers in their agricultural activities.
PRDP intends to examine the dynarnics pertaining to village group formation and functioning vis-a-vis
channel construction. It will also continue to encourage those farmers who obtain surplus in their
harvests to sell such, and will provide assistance in transporting their surplus to market. Lessons learned
will be shared with staff, who will then encourage successful group formation and participation in other
PRDP undertakings.
21.    This expansion of ARP activities will be possible through the corresponding development of
existing PRDP district offices. Additional district staff will be hired, so that an Engineer, Agriculturalist,
driver, secretary, and storeman are present in each. In addition, the provision of office equipment (e.g.
telephone, chairs/tables, file cabinet, computer/printer, generator, etc.) will enable these units to function
more efficiently and successfully coordinate ARP work.
MANAGEMENT
22.    Management of this Agricultural Reform Program (ARP) is carried out in at PRDP Headquarters
in Khorog, GBAO, by PRDP's Deputy General Manager. Technical aspects of the project will be the
responsibility of the Irrigation Engineer and the Agricultural Supervisor, with assistance from the district
field staff (Engineer and Agriculturalist). AKF, with its office in Dushanbe and its new Program Director
in Khorog, plays a supportive role and provides guidance on a number of issues.



- 82 -
1. PRDP has primary responsibility for the following:
a. implementation of ARP;
b. negotiations with local authorities and state farms for making land available for private
farming;
c. negotiations with individual or groups of farmers on agreements establishing the conditions
for provision by PRDP of agricultural inputs;
d. procurement of agricultural inputs and their distribution to contracting farmers;
e. verification of progress made under each agreement;
f. recruitment of PRDP program personnel and determination of conditions of employment
subject to agreed budgetary guidelines or limits;
g. preparing situation and implementation reports for AKF and donor agencies;
h. preparation of draft budgets.
2. AKF has primary responsibility for:
a. recruitment and terms and conditions of employment of consultants;
b. negotiating with PRDP management on the establishment of project budgets;
c. presentation of budgets to donor agencies;
d. provision of funds and payment of suppliers within agreed budgets;
e. financial control and auditing of PRDP accounts;
f. appointment of PRDP General Manager.
ENVIRONMENTAL ASPECTS
23.   PRDP's activities are being implemented in an environmentally sound way. Staff is clearly aware
of the fragile nature of the Pamir ecosystem, and they act accordingly. The proposed ARP will not have
adverse effects upon the enviroinent:
*     The small gravity-flow irritation channels will result in only the re-routing of water,
which will then flow into the same mountain rivers as prior to channel construction.
*     Trees and other vegetation will grow along the channels (as encouraged by PRDP), and
these "vegetative breaks" will help to stop erosion.
*     Channels such as these have been similarly constructed in GBAO for centuries, and both
the local populace and PRDP engineers/agriculturalists are well-versed in the technology.
*     Land to be brought under cultivation is not on the mountain-sides but rather on the edges
of existing fields and in valleys.
*     No displacement of communities will be involved.
*     The project will give farmers even more incentive to manage water sources carefully in
a stress environment and encourages group formation and participation at the village
level.

SUSTAINABILITY
24.   At present the entire population of Gorno-Badakhshan is the recipient of AKF-sponsored food
rations. In this on-going system of extreme dependence, PRDP's activities in ARP are crucial. They
will enable more farmers to avail of privately-managed land. They will become active decision-makers
in agriculture, not only individually but also in the context of village groups. Seed quality and crop
yields will improve, local markets will develop, and the potential for sustainable agricultural development
in GBAO will increase.
LINKS WITH TASIF
25.   As ARP is implemented, links between AKF/PRDP and TASIF will be strengthened. Through
joint activities in monitoring and evaluation, the institutions will engage in joint problem-solving. In
addition, in coordination with TASIF, PRDP will host interested government officials from throughout
the country and NGO staff from throughout the country in order to observe PRDP's progress to date and
learn from its experiences.
BUDGET
26.   The total budget for the Agricultural Reform Program is US$656,016: US$217,044 in 1997,
US$285,274 in 1998, and US$153,698 in 1999. More details are included in Annex M.
MONITORING AND REPORTING
27.   The recent placement of an AKF Program Director in Khorog will allow for on-going monitoring
of PRDP's activities, including ARP. Monitoring of district field staffs work is accomplished by
periodic visits by PRDP staff from the Khorog office. A system of careful field reporting in the district
offices is already in place, and this will be continued and strengthened through the further development
of these offices. Records are kept on each of the 32 channel construction schemes including applications
and negotiations of the village groups, distribution of materials, and the progress of channel construction
and land development. District office staff will submit detailed quarterly reports in writing to the PRDP
Head Office in Khorog, where summary reports will be compiled. Quarterly reports are submitted to
AKF offices in Khorog, Dushanbe, and Geneva. AKF/PRDP will submit quarterly reports on ARP
progress to TASIF, and a final report will be submitted after the close of the program.
28.   The key indicators to be monitored are: the number of kilometers of channel construction which
has been successfully completed, the number of schemes completed, and the number of hectares of land
brought under cultivation.



- 84 -
INTEGRATED EVALUATION
29.   An independent integrated evaluation at mid-term and project completion would be contracted by
TASIF to be undertaken by a consultant leading a multi-disciplinary team of local and international
experts. Implementing INGOs, including AKF, would each nominate a team member, but the team as
a whole would evaluate all aspects of both components of the project including the AKF Agricultural
Reform program. The team would visit project sites, review reports and indicator measurements in the
light of its own observations and investigations, and seek to establish lessons to be learned both at
mid-term and for Project follow-up. It would report both to TASIF and to the Bank. Further details
are in Annex L.
30.   Key objective indicators will be measured and monitored by the Project in order to track, in a
consistent and comparable fashion, the essentials of performance against objectives. The key objective
indicators are sununarized in Table 1 of Annex L. The first Project objective of impact on the incomes
of the poor will be measured across all sub-components by five key indicators, of which two will indicate
the extent to which this objective has been met in a cost effective and participatory fashion. In addition,
each sub-component will be evaluated according to three specific indicators relating to the particular
program. For the AKF program these three specific indicators are: channels completed and command area
realized; land area developed for production, households benefiting; and change in food productivity and
production. These indicators measure impact, coverage and sustainability. The second Project objective
of capability building would be measured by two general indicators across all components, with a third
relating specifically to the training of TASIF staff.  Thus each sub-component will be evaluated on ten
indicators, of which seven are common to all sub-components of the Project, and three are specific to
each sub-component.  These indicators, and the specific measures and instruments to compile them,
would be refined in a pilot baseline exercise.
31.   Monitoring and evaluation activities are already a feature of the existing agricultural reform
program, and both the findings and systems used will be built upon in the scaled-up program. The Project
would closely monitor the scaled-up program. Specifically, SCF-US would be asked to report to the
TASIF on a quarterly basis on the following: (i) summary of achievements: number of beneficiaries
reached (direct and indirect) versus goal; cost per beneficiary (total NGO program cost divided by
number of beneficiaries); labor content as a percentage of the NGO program budget; number of person-
days of employment generated; and operating costs as a percentage of the NGO program budget; (ii)
obstacles and issues to be overcome and resolved; (iii) targeting; (iv) community participation; (v)
financial reporting; and, (vi) sustainability. Each of the NGO programs would also have a monitoring
schedule based on its specific characteristics (see paras. 27-28). In addition to this, the AKF will report
to TASIF on achieved targets in the agriculture reform program in order to trigger release of payments
by TASIF to AKF. Payments from TASIF to AKF will be made in five tranches of 10%, 20%, 20%,
40% and 10%. The first payment of 10% will be an advance. The four subsequent payments will be
made against achieved targets which are given below:
End of June 1997: Second Payment of 20%
- First planning workshop for ARP staff held in April
- 6 district offices in the process of enlarging/strengthening (each being staffed with engineer,

ASSUMPTIONS AND RISKS
30.    Humanitarian Aid: Presently the whole population of Gorno-Badakhshan receives humanitarian
aid from international donors in the form of food rations organized by AKF. It is assumed that this donor
assistance will continue (and slowly be phased out) as Pamiris move towards self-sufficiency in the area
of agriculture.
31.   Political Instability: Political instability on the road from Khorog to Dushanbe has led to its
periodic closing, the construction of numerous check-posts, etc. This has direct repercussions upon
Gorno-Badakhshan concerning transport, trade, etc. Relations between the Pamiri population and the
national government are also strained. In addition, the border with Afghanistan is tense. Risks of
conflict are constant, but it is assumed that stability will prevail and program activities will continue.
32.    Seasonal Variation: Another risk concerns the weather in Gorno-Badakhshan. It is assumed that
the seasons, rainfall, and other related conditions will not be harsh and that crops will be forthcoming.



- 86 -
ANNEX F
REPUBLIC OF TAJIKISTAN
PILOT POVERTY ALLEVIATION PROJECT
SUMMARY OF SOCIAL ASSESSMENTt'
INTRODUCTION
1.      A Social Assessment (SA) was been undertaken in order better to understand the nature and
causes of poverty, its dynamics, and the context in which effective solutions may be developed. Although
the SA is best seen as a process of investigation, its central part is a field investigation conducted in May-
June 1996 in seven selected sites, to address the following key questions:
Poverty Profre:
3 Who is poor, where, how and why?
Livelihood strategies:
3 What are people doing?
3 How to meet their basic needs?
Elements for a response:
* What are local priority needs for poverty alleviation?
3 What is local capacity to organize for development?
3 What is the community assessment of existing NGO programs?
3 What are the implications for a Social Investment Fund?
2.      The field study method was based on Participatory Rapid Appraisal which seeks to document local
knowledge of problems and their solutions. Qualitative research was chosen in preference to quantitative
methods at this stage, because the rapidly changing situation in Tajikistan requires an understanding of
the dynamics of change before indicators can be developed for the study of quantitative aspects.
8/ The social assessment report was compiled and written by Ms E. Gomart, consultant to the World Bank, who also trained
and led the research team and analyzed the results of their findings. A research team of 11 Tajik nationals undertook five weeks
of intensive field work in May-June 1996, working extremely long hours and displaying a high level of commitment, a
willingness to learn new research techniques, and an ability to learn about conditions and trends in their country. The team
consisted of Mokhabad Akhmedova; Atoyev Kamol; Abdugafur Khaidarov; Khosanev; P. Kh. Khudaidadov; Shakhia Sh.
Patchadjanova; Maksuda Sanginovna; Firuz C. Saydov; M.H. Tursunov; Davlatmo Yusufbekova; Djema Yusupdjanova. Raisa
Mukhutdinova assisted with translation. Mark Waite led the research team in Gorno-Badakshan, and also edited and translated
the field reports. Government officials at all sites were generous with their time. International NGOs provided ample
information to a demanding schedule, and assisted the field team with logistics. Generous assistance in many ways was provided
by the Center for Strategic Research, the Aid Coordination Unit and the World Bank Liaison Office. Keith Rennie (EMTEN)
provided technical and editorial advice, and the task was managed by Michael Mills (EC3HR).



- 87 -
POVERTY PROFILE
A.    Background
3.    Even before the war, Tajikistan was one of the poorest newly independent states of the former
Soviet Union. It inherited low levels of investment, an imbalanced pattern of industrialization, high
dependency on imported food, and weak social indicators (high malnutrition rates, low school attendance
of girls in most rural areas, high unemployment). Since independence in 1991, chronic poverty was
rapidly worsened both by the collapse of the union economy and by the civil war which caused an
estimated 25,000 deaths and 600,000 refugees and displaced people--mostly from Kurgan Tube region.
Although the civil war is said to have ended in October 1993, fighting erupts periodically and opposition
forces are active in the central-eastern part of the country: in the Garm Valley, and the pre-Pamir districts
of Darwaz and Vanj.
4.    Most households have been hard hit hard by the civil war and protracted political and economic
crises. As a result, security and rule of law are main priorities for the population. There is a widespread
desire for peace, for an end to forced conscription, improved security and increased rule of law. Political
and military meddling by outsiders is seen as a main impediment to normalization. Most people believe
peace may be achieved either by the coming to power of a third neutral political party, or by a coalition
government that will bring together all political factions.
B.    Who are the Poor?
5.    Some communities identified three socio-economic strata: the poorest households, those who were
average, and the rich. Other communities were more sharply divided between a few well-to-do families
and the rest of the population who were "spending their days running after a piece of bread". Some
effort was required to overcome initial reluctance to reveal socio-economic distinctions within a
community, and to cope with local perceptions of the possible motives of outside researchers.
6.    Informants identified the poor as those who, through a combination of adverse factors, are
struggling to put bread on the table. The poor are those who in the past relied the most on allowances
and other forms of subsidies during the Soviet Union. Poor families tend to have no working man; many
children (eight or more); no land, or just a small area often planted only with wheat; insufficient
agricultural inputs; and poor state of health. The poor include those families who rely overly on salaries
from the kolkhoz (i.e. the man works on the kolkhoz), social services or unskilled labor (e.g. cleaner).



- 88 -
7.     The middle stratum of society includes those people who need to work hard and use all their
resources to make ends meet. They usually have at least two males of working age, and thus, in
comparison to poor families, have a higher ratio of males compared to females, children and elderly.
These families also have access to land which they are cultivating. They may hold two or more cows
(or one cow, plus sheep or goats). Cash remittances from family members working elsewhere are
important. Within this group, the households that are doing rather better are those that have a resource
cushion which they have been able to accumulate or to preserve. This cushion consists of savings (e.g.
cattle or capital), or productive assets (e.g. orchards or equipment) which allow the household to survive
a period of adversity. By contrast, most people who find themselves towards the bottom of this middle
stratum are conscious of the risk that they may fall into poverty. They are relatively poor and are able
to meet only the most basic food and clothing needs. The solutions that they have found to their
problems are vulnerable to being nullified at any time by corruption, insecurity, and market fluctuations.
8.    In general, the rich are those who have a link to the management of local industries, to local or
regional government, or to the security forces. In towns and large district centers, respondents perceived
that the rich gain a living not so much from their work itself, but through benefits obtained by virtue of
their position of employment. Another source of wealth is large remittances from Russia.
C.    Local Criteria of Poverty
9.    Communities identified the following indicators of relative poverty between households. These
are:
*  inability to provide basic household needs: food, shelter, shoes, health, education;
*  no labor, or insufficient labor, available at household level;
*  depletion of assets (cattle, personal property);
*  lack of access to productive assets;
*  lack of employment; and
*  lack of ties to the local ruling clan
D.    Basic Needs
10.   Many households were not able to satisfy more than basic food needs. The quality and quantity
of food available was usually assessed by the type of bread available for household consumption, since
bread quality corresponds closely to household incomes and access to cash and markets. This spring
(1996) many households had to eke out scarce supplies of wheat flour by mixing it with other grains--
maize, and even hay and cotton seeds. Some families had no wheat flour at all, and were using flour
which they made only from hay or other grasses. A few infants were said to have died of hunger in the
villages in the week preceding the survey, and many adults and children were extremely thin and faint
with hunger.



- 89 -
11.    Shelter is a need that cannot be met by many resettled households who have few assets and
sources of cash income. Shelter is also a problem for young families, and results in overcrowding in
single rooms and on plots for single households.
12.    The lack of children's shoes is the most often cited obstacle to school attendance. After food,
shoes are the next the main cash purchase that poor families make. Clothes are also needed among
recently displaced or resettled families who often have left their homes empty-handed.
13.    Poor access to health services was also an indicator of poverty, both because it implies reduced
capacity to generate income, and because it is associated with poor nutrition. Health services are
increasingly out of reach for the poor. Not only are medicines routinely sold, but doctors--especially in
towns--are charging for their services. In villages, doctors have so few resources that treatment is
commonly unavailable. The poor reported that increasingly doctors are turning away poor patients
because they cannot pay for care. As a result, most families try to turn to tabibs, traditional healers who
are found in most villages and towns.
14.   Poor access to education is linked to low incomes, as demonstrated through the lack of shoes,
winter clothes, supplies, transportation and reliance on incomes earned by children. Poor education
uptake also derives from the declining value of education in the eyes of parents, and to tradition which
limits attendance of girls over the age of 11. In urban areas, where schools collect money for repairs,
fees are also increasingly being charged by teachers. The exodus of teachers and decreased attendance
of teachers during the school day also jeopardizes the quality of education.
E.    Labor power
15.    Labor power is literally manpower, and refers to the number of healthy, grown men capable of
providing for the household. The criterion speaks to the felt reality of female-headed households:
*  women without husbands often lack the confidence to request officials for land, nor do they
receive the respect which would lead to the request's being granted;
*  strong workers are needed to clear boulders from reclaimed land;
*  the more male adults compared to the rest of the members of the household, the better off
the household;
*  work in the CIS is mostly physical work, such as construction and agricultural work, which
requites strong, healthy men.
16.    Manpower is threatened by forced conscription which keeps many young men out of the markets
and the fields, and by continued insecurity in areas of former conflict, which impedes the return of
husbands and grown men to these areas.
F.    Depletion of assets
17.    Cattle holdings in rural areas are a store for wealth. There is occurring throughout the country
a rapid and dramatic decline in cattle holdings of formerly "average" (two-cow) families, meaning a



- 90 -
depletion of household savings. Cattle are being sold more rapidly than the rate of natural replacement
in order to purchase flour (especially during the spring) and medicines.
18.    The poor, especially displaced and resettled families and Russian pensioners in towns, are selling
their personal belongings, including refrigerator, furniture, dishes, blankets, and even the wooden floor
planks.
G.    Lack of access to productive assets
19.   Most people hope to cultivate land as a viable local source of income. Land use and leasing is
accorded by law to all rural residents. Land leases are meant to be long-term, with the crop to be
determined by the individual lease-holder. However, in practice access to land is determined less by
residence and more by other factors including: manpower in the household; profession (kolkhoz worker,
farm management, teacher or doctor); regional-ethnic group; availability of resources for cultivation;
quality, and distance of land relative to place of residence; and access to markets. Access to land and
the ability to cultivate it are often reserved for the well-to-do.
20.    The type of cultivation on household plots is a good indicator of resources available for income
generation and access to markets. Cultivation of presidential land which varies greatly in terms of
quality, distance from the village, water supply, etc., can also provide a view into resources -- material
or political to secure and cultivate a favorable plot. The type of land lease obtained by the household
may also reveal its standing within the kolkhoz. The common kolkhoz worker commonly receives one-
year lease for a comparatively small area (e.g. 1-5 hectares, or 100 apple trees) with a predetermined
crop, and a price to be determined on an individual basis at the time of harvest. The well-to-do lease-
holder receives a long-term lease (5-10 years) for a comparatively large area (5 to 600 hectares) with no
predetermined crop.
21.    Access to other types of productive assets such as equipment (tractors, trucks, buses and vans)
is also a good indicator of wealth. Mostly tractors are owned by rural residents, while mechanized
transportation, such as buses and vans, are rented from the city authority and require an agreement with
the town to operate.
H.    Employment
22.    In both urban and rural areas an important criteria of poverty is employment--not only its access
but its type. Heads of households working in the collective farm were considered the poorest. Doctors
and especially teachers in villages were also seen as poor. Those with employment which provides access
to materials and products that can be used or sold on the market were considered the best off--these
included enterprise, farm and government officials. Salaries also vary greatly between sectors. For
example, one farm director earns TR 13,000, and a policeman in Shakhristan, Leninabad region, was said
to make TR 20,000 per month, while the local teacher made on average TR 1,200.



- 91 -
I.     Ties to the local ruling clan
23.     Access to resources for wealth may be a function of membership in a localized clan. Such clans
may dominate entire bureaucracies at the local and regional levels. The case of the village of Galadzor
in Vose was striking. One village consisted of "foreigners" originally from Hovalling. It was
systematically disadvantaged in relation to neighboring Vose-originated villages in the rest of the kolkhoz,
in respect of land distribution, availability of pasture land and employment opportunities as hired labor.
J.     Predisposing Characteristics: Rural, War-affected, and Isolated.
24.   Based on the above criteria we can conclude that three main characteristics render certain areas
liable to poverty: a rural situation, in contrast to an urban one; war-affected villages, and isolated
villages.
25.    Urban areas are less vulnerable to poverty because they present opportunities for employment as
well as access to markets. The poverty of some urban households may be no less deep than that of rural
ones, but it is less widespread. The most vulnerable in towns are Russians, and single pensioners, and
female-headed households without a bread winner. Among the poorest urban households were also those
headed by unemployed, unskilled men.
War affected villages are most at risk from:
-  a high proportion of female-headed families with low levels of man power and poor access
to land;
e  low levels of savings (cattle) that act as a buffer against hunger;
i  low nutritional status because of lack of means for cultivating cash crops, eroded assets
(cattle, personal goods), resulting in poor health status;
*  poor condition or lack of shelter because of destruction related to the war and erosion of
savings (construction materials, cash);
*  discrimination and lack of security which inhibits growth and affects particularly those
originally from Garm and the Pamirs;
*  low level of attendance of schools owing to low incomes and fear of reprisals.
26.   Isolated villages which are located over 10 km from an active market or which do not have a
tradition of cultivating cash crops have been hard hit hard by price increases and by the decrease in real
value of salaries. The situation was caused by the following factors:
* Land problems: non-use of Presidential Land; lack of access to leased land; squeezing of
small farmers off irrigated land by large lease-holders, and from access to pasture lands
* rapidly decreasing level of cattle holdings



- 92 -
* lack of cultivation for market sales
* low level of access to education: affecting mostly girls over 12
* low level of health services provided in villages
27.   In these villages, a solution to the problem was not immediately visible and contributed to the
feeling of hopelessness.
LIVELIHOOD STRATEGIES
28.    How do households and individuals cope with these problems? Throughout the different sites,
several trends were identified:
decreased consumption levels
diversification of income sources (away from salaries, and toward land cultivation)
increased participation of all members of the household (women, children)
increased incidence of begging for and stealing of food products.
29.    Some strategies are common to all situations, while others are more specific to different
situations.



- 93 -

A.    Common Livelihood Strategies
30.   Lower consumption: Reduced consumption is a strategy that has been adopted by the great
majority of households in Tajikistan to live through this crisis. Reduction in consumption has limited
expenditures ancd consumption of home produced goods to the basics. Food quantity and quality has been
affected. The use of corn or hay for bread, mentioned above, is one example. Households also reduce
purchases such as meat, oil and sugar to under 10 per cent of their budget. Ceremonies--such as
weddings and funerals--have also been curtailed so that poor families can conduct them.
31.   Support networks: Networks of support are very effective, whether these are based on relatives
or a wider community or neighborhood. For example one pensioner living alone in Dushanbe said that
she relied on her neighbors and relatives for 80 percent of her needs. Within households, the work force



- 94 -
as well as income are commonly pooled, making use of economies of scale and attempting to lower the
dependency ratio. In rural areas, all members of the household, including women and children,
participate in production.
32.    Humanitarian assistance is an essential source of food for poor households in the war-affected
areas and in Gorno Badakhshan.
33.   Remittances are a strategy common to both urban and rural areas with a tradition of sending men
abroad for seasonal jobs. It remains the main strategy for alleviating local unemployment, especially
among young men. Access to this strategy requires the following: a large village or nearby center to
facilitate transportation; a network of ethnic or regional contacts in the CIS; a local tradition of seasonal
labor; and some capital for placement fee, transport and initial expenses. While remittances provide the
main source of income to build up savings, to purchase assets and to rehabilitate war-affected villages,
they do not provide a reliable source of income to cover daily expenses, leaving families sometimes at
risk.
B.    Livelihood Strategies in Villages
34.    Land provides the most reliable source of income to rural households. However access to
sufficient land and essential inputs is limited. As a result, grain production from Presidential land and
household plots satisfies the needs of the household for only 3-6 months of the year. Consequently,
households need to diversify production to more lucrative produce which can be exchanged for grain.
Cash crops were principally sold in small amounts because of difficulties with transport and physical
security. Land leases, as seen previously, do not necessarily provide a good source of income. As a
result, farmers are also informally cultivating land: by planting pasture land, and by double-cropping on
leased land. For example, wheat is planted in leased orchards.
35.    Cattle sales is the main livelihood strategy of the poor in mountainous and isolated villages,
although it depletes a major asset. For those who are able to manage sales in such a way as to avoid this
danger, cattle trade can be an excellent source of income.
36.   Markets: Town markets are a place where rural residents sell home-produced fruits, vegetables,
milk products or meat either directly, or in bulk to a trader. Access to market is a is influenced by:
*  security;
*  distances between the village and the nearest market;
*  local production and demand for home produced goods;
*  costs of production and marketing;
* gender; and
*  access to transport (cart, tractor, truck).



- 95 -
37.   In villages there are no proper markets. Urban based middlemen sometimes come to buy produce
in villages with trucks or cars. Itinerant salesmen also go from house to house to sell or barter goods.
38.    Trade and self-employment in villages consists of petty trade stalls, mostly operated by children
and young women; production of crafts and other sewing (women); renting out of machinery; and private
trading in cattle. Skilled labor in villages, such as carpenters, has additional opportunities for earning
incomes either locally or in other CIS countries. Unskilled labor from the villages travels to the towns
to compete for odd-jobs, or to work the land of large lease-holders. These opportunities are few and far
between, and cannot be a reliable source of income for a household.
39.    Salaries, especially rural salaries, rarely provide an important proportion of household income.
Already low, salaries have been considerably eroded by inflation, and in any case are rarely paid--in one
site, the latest payment was in 1995, while in another there has been no payment since 1994. Yet farmers
receive partial payments in kind. Small amounts of food, fuel wood or cotton stocks are the incentive
to work. Additional income generation activities are also available through the farm such as silkworm
production which is lucrative yet requires a lot of labor. Doctors and teachers receive such small salaries
that they are commonly considered some of the poorest in the village. Town and farm officials on the
other hand, as well as security or army personnel are well paid.
40.    Gathering of medicinal herbs is an additional source of income, especially for pensioners and
women. Yet it provides only small contributions to household income.
41.    Begging and stealing are common among villagers. One pensioner estimated that begging in
distant villages represents 15 percent of her income. Stealing food is another source of income which
is increasing in importance especially in the spring when people have run out of food. This is sometimes
done openly. Stealing fruit and other cash crops for sale at the market was also a main source of income
during Soviet times.
C.    Livelihood Strategies in Towns
42.   In towns the livelihood strategies differed greatly from those in the villages. The main trends in
livelihood strategies in towns and district centers were:
*  diversifying income sources from the main salary to other cash sources;
*  holding more than one salaried job simultaneously;
*  acquiring equipment, transportation or using acquired skills;
* involving all persons in the household in income generation (including children);
* links to agricultural production
43.    Salaried employment remains the preferred source of income for many urban residents, often
being supplemented by other activities (such as trading), earnings from odd jobs, or from salaries from
other formal employment.



- 96 -
44.    While many salaries are low and often unpaid, the following are incentives:
* high salaries in certain industries or sectors (such as security);
* access to equipment, fuel and raw materials for sale or private production;
* associated privileges (housing, free utilities, kindergarten, polyclinic).
45.    Others have lost out in the crisis of salaries, particularly:
* teachers, health workers and other workers in social services;
* unqualified labor;
* workers in uncompetitive industries (i.e. those using raw materials purchased from abroad,
producing low quality products at high costs).
46.    Market activities: In the main towns, large markets attract goods from the surrounding area, and
also from Dushanbe and neighboring countries. Markets are considered to be the main source of income
for urban households. Most industry and social services employees were said to be also involved in
market activities, usually in petty or small trade, before or after their working hours.
47.    Access to the market is limited by:
* physical insecurity, which limits the scale of activities;
* military conscription, which keeps young men away from public places;
* cost of access to market as formal and informal fees to be paid to officials; and
* availability of start-up capital.
48.    Petty trading, where the seller resells goods from the central market to secondary markets,
provides only minimal amounts of daily income, depending on the scale of the operation. Usually all
family members are involved, with the adults purchasing goods and the women and children selling.
There is also small trade in goods stolen from factories or products made from materials obtained through
the place of formal employment. One woman estimated that 60 per cent of her income derives from
dresses she makes from materials stolen at the textile factory. Urban sellers also purchase agricultural
products in bulk from villagers in town or go to nearby villages to purchase goods in bulk. This is a
more viable source of income.
49.    Medium-scale trading applies to products procured from nearby countries, such as CIS, central
Asian republics, Pakistan, China, or the United Arab Emirates; or through intermediary markets such
as Termez, Dushanbe and Ura Tube. Women tend to trade in clothes and household appliances and to
travel by plane and bus for reasons of security. Men, on the other hand, tend to travel by road or rail
and to trade mostly in bulk quantities of food and fuel. The main obstacle to scaling up trade is the need
to secure protection and capital. As a result, certain groups are most likely to be involved in trading of



- 97 -
imported goods, either because they have access to capital (e.g. Urgut Uzbeks) or links to the security
forces.
50.   Wage Labor: Daily wage labor is common in cities. Near the bazaars, large groups of 40-50
men from the town or from the nearby villages stand around hoping for offers of work. Men working
in factories also work as wage labor at week-ends or during their free time. Young boys and men also
stand around with carts waiting for a job.
PROJECT IMPLICATIONS
A.    Priorities, Resources and Opportunities
The main priorities for poverty alleviation are:
*  Peace, security and rule of law;
*  Improved ability to meet basic needs: food shelter, shoes, health education;
*  Diversity of income sources;
*  Access to productive assets to increase self-sufficiency and the ability to produce tradeable
goods.
51.   Peace, security and the rule of law: The desire for peace was widely expressed across the sites
surveyed. People believed that with peace, the economic and political situation would improve and so
would physical security. Physical and psychological insecurity is felt most acutely in war-affected areas
(Kurgan Tube and Garm) and by certain groups, namely Garmis and Pamiris. Women in these areas feel
particularly vulnerable without husbands or grown men to protect them. More general feelings of
insecurity, which were encountered in many regions from Shakhristan to GAO, also inhibit economic
rehabilitation and economic exchange and development. Police and border guards often demand bribes
or part of the produce being transported. Food security is adversely affected when armed men steal
harvests. Peace might also enable the borders with Uzbekistan to be reopened, and bring increased
market and job opportunities. The continued conflict and forced conscription also restricts the mobility
of young men who avoid public places. The lack of rule of law has had a major impact on resource
allocation, favoring ruling clans at the expense of others.
52.   Ability to meet basic needs. To assist the many households throughout the country that are
currently unable to meet their most basic food needs, the following types of activities are proposed:
*  Information dissemination about laws and regulations regarding resource allocation, especially
privatization, in order to improve people's knowledge of their own rights;
*  Material inputs (e.g. seeds, water systems) to assist the poor effectively to exploit those
resources to which they have access;
*  Material assistance to support families in meeting their immediate food and shelter needs;



- 98 -
Development of cash crops and other marketable products through the provision of capital
and material inputs to improve household incomes and therefore access to staples (flour) and
other cash-based needs.
53.    Diversification of sources of income. Diversification of household incomes from dependence
on inadequate salaries towards inclusion of an array of in-kind and cash incomes is the main trend in
livelihood strategies. It can be effectively supported through small-scale savings and credit schemes
targeted to the poor.
54.   Improved access to productive assets, such as land and cattle, is central to enable the poor to
provide for their own needs. Asset allocation that consciously enables their access by the poor, along
with support for the necessary inputs, will permit a more effective exploitation of these resources. A key
requirement is a system of allocation and access that provides long-term security of use and encourages
long-term investment.
B.    Local Institutions, Informal and Formal
55.    Local institutions are structures within which local resources for development can be mobilized.
Institutions working effectively to support households and communities are for the most part informal,
traditional, male dominated, and based on family relations or locality, and often have a religious element.
Formal institutions, such as the Women's Councils or Youth League, are center-based, hierarchical and
top-down.  The survey found that the presence and activities of the formal institutions in local
communities was not always as great as their representatives believed. Despite their limitations, a locally-
based participatory poverty alleviation strategy will have to take account of the limitations and potentials
of both informal and formal institutions and either work through them or at least ensure their passive
support.
56.    The extended family network, in a patriarchal society, centers on the male head of household,
his household and that of his sons. The clan which is based both on family and locality, includes the
immediate relatives of the male head of household.
57.    Neighborhood networks, based on the principle of residential origin, are the second major type
of local network. People who have neither family ties nor do they come from the same village or
neighborhood, are deemed "foreigners." Thus the historical demography of a neighborhood or village
is very important as a basis for understanding systems of resource allocation.
58.    The mosque, Mullah and elders are well respected, informal leaders in the community. The
Mullah and the group of elders can play an active role in programming through information dissemination
to the population; mobilizing resources for programs; monitoring quality and timely execution and conflict
resolution.
59.    Female counterparts to the Mullah--Bibikhallifah--are found both in towns and villages. Their
main role is the religious education of women and performing religious ceremonies with women. More
information is needed on other roles they play in communities of women.



- 99 -
60.   The Mahallah representative or Hudjain in villages is an informal leader elected by the
community. The representative acts as a liaison to the formal local government structures. This type
of local leader can be found in those areas not affected by the war. In GAO, it was found that the
Hudjain works through a council, including one woman.
61.    Representatives of formal institutions should not be expected to represent the communities since
they are appointed from above and often are new arrivals from a different region and/or sector. These
include: the town, district and regional representatives and farm officials. Other formal institutions such
as the Women's Councils, the Youth League, etc., were found to be operating, if at all, then at a smaller
scale than in the past. Since the demise of the Soviet Union, some of these had reformed as different
entities but retained old links, such as the Youth League (formerly the Komsomol--i.e. Communist Youth
League) which retains funding links to the Communist Party.
62.    The SA found considerable local support for a participatory style of development. Communities
would like to be involved program design. Elders are eager to play an active role in program monitoring,
mobilization of resources. Local monitors should be independent from government, particularly in
matters of resource allocation Information dissemination should be improved by using traditional leaders
(Mullah, elders) and publicity at communal centers.
C.    Implications for Regional Focus
63.    The current crisis and internal conflict has had different effects according to regions. The table
below summarizes the main characteristics, opportunities and resources, and suggests a tentative
prioritization by poverty criteria.
D.    Possible Social Investment Fund micro-projects
64.    A number of ideas for projects arose during discussions with local officials, and other community
members. Some of these have already been planned for or are partially constructed, others are programs
which would allow the community to adjust better to the current situation.
65.    The kinds of projects for which community proposals are likely to be developed are:
Social infrastructure
Waste water canal cleaning
Irrigation repair
Pump replacement
Small water and sanitation stations
Public baths and latrines
Small hydroelectric plants
Reconstruction of schools and health points, especially in war-affected or distant villages
Kindergarten repairs or construction
Reclaiming land: new irrigation canals, clearing rocks
Constructing community storage for fruits and vegetables



- 100-
Environmental Projects
Reforestation
Terracing
Flood and mud-slide prevention
Enterprise development
Investment in enterprises--processing of local products
Small loans to micro-enterprises
Employment for women
Small factories: shoes, food processing
Rebuilding of greenhouses for lemons
66.   Programs should be aware of traditional gender roles which often define activities in which
women are willing to take part. Frequently, married and unmarried women alike say that they are not
comfortable going to the market or working outside the village.
67.   Program design should not rely on unverified official representation of the needs and priorities
of the population. Officials are often new to the region and unfamiliar with or remote from popular
concerns. At the same time, the program should be aware of the sensitivities of officials, who may
perceive a poverty alleviation program as evidence of the failure of their policies and management; and
it should seek to develop their positive support.
