ANNEX K
REPUBLIC OF TAJIKISTAN
PILOT POVERTY ALLEVIATION PROJECT
Review of NGO Activities in Tajikistan
based on a Desk Study of Reports and other Documents
by Lawrence S. Cumming
BACKGROUND
1.       This review focuses primarily on NGOs which have an interest and programming
capacity that has an economic as well as a social character and impact.(See table 1 for a summary
of all International NGO programs in Tajikistan)
OVERVIEW OF NGO SECTOR
2.        This report deals more with foreign than with national or local NGOs. All available
evidence suggests that local NGOs, to the extent that they exist, tend to be rather new, small and
lacking in experience. Therefore, they would not be in a position to implement projects funded
under the PPAP.
3.        The pre-eminent exception to this rule would be the Pamir Relief and Development
Program (PRDP) which was established by the Aga Khan Foundation. Though young, it operates
with a level of resources not enjoyed by any other Tajikistan NGO. Another exception might be
the national Red Crescent Society which is described as the largest domestic NGO in Tajikistan,
in terms of its membership, with chapters throughout the country.
4.    This fact points up a dilemma for the Bank. On the one hand, circumstances suggest the
need for supporting activities likely to have relatively quick results. On the other, it would be
desirable to build national and local NGO capacity for the longer run. This dilemma might best
be resolved by giving priority support to those international NGOs which demonstrate the most
serious evidence of involvement in building partnership with and in strengthening local and
national NGO bodies.
A.    Networks Between/Among NGOs: In-country Networks
5.    In the wake of the 1992-93 civil war, NGOs and donors working in Tajikistan began to
meet regularly and formed a committee of international organizations. It currently meets every
Monday morning. This initiative was previously convened by the UNHCR but is now being



- 125-
chaired by UNDP. The purpose of this grouping is to share information and, wherever possible,
to co-ordinate action. Apart from the regular weekly donors meeting, the foreign NGOs meet
once a month to discuss issues of concern to them.
6.    There is no indigenous NGO coordinating organization in Tajikistan, though there is an
NGO Resource Center which Counterpart Consortium (CC) has assisted. This is housed in the
same building as Counterpart and shares facilities with that organization.
7.    Counterpart and Save the Children (US) jointly support the NGO Capacity Building
Project which is a training scheme for NGO staff, both those associated with local NGOs and the
locally engaged Tajik staff of foreign NGOs.
B.    Networks in the Region or Beyond
8.    The Project for Economic Reform and Development in Central Asia (PERDCA), though
not itself an NGO, maintains an NGO support function in Central Asia, assisting NGOs to
develop regional contacts. One of its services is Silknet, an electronic network connecting
organizations in Tajikistan and neighboring republics. With assistance from the Eurasia
Foundation and the Soros Foundation, it will continue to install sub-nodes throughout the region.
9.    Counterpart Consortium (CC), with USAID assistance, maintains the Nongovernmental
Organization Support Initiative for Central Asia. (It now has a Tajikistan office and, as noted
above, is actively involved in training.) CC offers training for indigenous NGOs (management
and sustainability), promotes and facilitates information exchange and partnerships and offers a
seed grants program, including "partnership grants" for strengthening capacity to serve members
and "corporate challenge grants" which encourage corporations to contribute matching monies.
To the end of 1995, the Consortium reports that more than 300 Central Asian NGOs have
received training, that a region-wide data base on local NGOs has been formed and is being
continuously updated and that forty nine (49) grants totalling over $350,000 have been awarded
throughout the region.
C.    Current/Planned NGO Projects: Refugee Assistance
10.   Due to the conditions resulting from the civil war (1992-93), the UNHCR has played a
leading role in humanitarian aid to Tajikistan to assist returning refugees and other displaced
persons.
11.    Some examples of NGO assistance to refugees include:
*       a study of the needs of refugees, with particular reference to water and
sanitation which the IRC conducted in August-September, 1993.
*       the Aga Khan Foundation/PRDP humanitarian relief effort. One evaluation
characterized the AKF feeding program as having "literally rescued the
population of Gorno-Badakshan from the threat of famine" and, therefore,
as one of the outstanding examples of NGO response.



- 126 -
*       the SCF (US) assisted some 62,000 refugees and internally displaced persons
in the wake of the civil war to rebuild and rehabilitate housing whilst feeding
some 27,000 brigade workers through food for work projects.
D.      Micro-enterprise/Credit and Small Enterprise Development
12.     The Tajikistan Government has defined micro-enterprises as those having up to nine
(9) employees and small enterprises as having between ten (10) and one hundred and ninety-nine
(199) employees.
13.     Some examples are as follows:
*       SCF (U.S.) has been supporting a group guaranteed lending program
(GGLP) at the village level in Khatlon Region (patterned after the ASA
Program and Grameen Bank in Bangladesh) and has engaged in training for
small enterprise development for women and their families as well as small
business entrepreneurs. The early experience of this program has been quite
positive; for instance, there has been a zero default rate on loans.
i       SCF (U.K.) has sponsored small enterprise development through a
monetization scheme and small scale income generation projects. These have
been specifically directed towards female headed households.
-       The Soros Foundation has provided financial assistance to both SCF (U.S.)
and SCF (U.K.) for their micro-enterprise and credit schemes.
For a period of about five months in early 1995, Mercy Corps International
(MCI) sponsored monetization projects using food donated by the U.S.
Department of Agriculture. Through the mechanism of third-party
agreements, all credits extended under such projects are repaid to local non-
profit organizations and not to MCI.
*       Relief International (RI) has made a contribution through quick impact
projects (QIPs) focusing on women heads of families. All 1,350 women who
had participated in the QIPs subsequently were offered training. Of these,
five hundred and forty (540) had done so by the end of 1995. Ninety percent
(90%) of business plan proposals (goat, sheep and poultry breeding, bread
making, sewing, knitting, soap making and so on) had been accepted as of
that time.
*       Shelter Now International (SNI) has supported capacity in small enterprise
development through on-the-job training and the provision of building
products.
*       Various NGOs have reported on projects which produce goods and materials.
IRC, for instance, has been supporting the rehabilitation of small soap and
shoe production industries.



- 127-
*        MCI has made loans to agricultural enterprises and reports quite favorable
results.
*       AED, in partnership with the US Embassy/Department of Commerce, has
established a Business Initiative Resource Center, though this would appear
to be somewhat beyond the scope of this study. It also, reportedly, benefits
larger businesses rather than small and micro enterprises.
E.       Rehabilitation/Reconstruction - Public Works and Housing
14.     The fact that so many NGOs are involved in public works and the reconstruction and
rehabilitation of physical infrastructure and property is, of course, a direct consequence of the
1992-93 civil war and the conditions under which they first began their Tajikistan programs.
Some examples are:
*       SCF (U.S) took over physical reconstruction from UNHCR (which had
initiated the program). This food for work (FFW) activity is now being
supported by USAID. SCF (US) has been involved in housing and schools
reconstruction projects as well as the clearing of rubble and building of
irrigation ditches, primarily in West Khatlon. It is also providing plastic
sheeting for fruit greenhouses.
e       MCI has taken on both free food distribution and FFW  projects (e.g.
cleaning drainage systems and parks and public facilities in Dushanbe).
e       CARITAS has also been involved in a substantial way (with $1,250,000
funding from European sources) in reconstruction of returning refugees'
houses and the production of housing tiles.
*       The International Organization for Migration (IOM) is involved in village
level disaster prevention and mitigation as well as reconstruction and
rehabilitation in landslide prone mountainous areas of the Panjkent region.
It has co-operated with SCF (US) as well as with SNI in housing
reconstruction. Because of the interest of a company operating a gold mine
in the area in improving its public relations situation, IOM has been able to
involve this firm in a constructive way in support of the project. (Though,
strictly speaking an intergovernmental organization, IOM has a number of
NGO characteristics.)
*       Shelter Now International (SNI) is operating a demonstration facility to
produce appropriate building materials, notably roofing tiles which, it hopes,
will be both less expensive in the long run and safer than the asbestos
sheeting of which much is used.
F.      Water and Sanitation
15.     Water and sanitation projects figure prominently in the programs of the IRC and



- 128-
UNICEF which work as partners in this area. IRC took over a project in the fall of 1994 begun
by Medecins Sans Frontiers (MSF) Belgium and started another such project in Khatlon with
USAID assistance.
16.     A recent evaluation concluded that, although the IRC project had been carried out well,
it did not meet its full potential from a community development point of view. Moreover, it noted
that impact assessment is lacking. This fact may, perhaps, be due in part to IRC's historic
emphasis on relief.
G.      Agriculture, Food Security and Rural Development
17.     Several NGO projects fall under the heading of agricultural development:
*       The AKF/PRDP has a large program of assistance to farmers in Gorno-
Badakshan and has moved from its initial humanitarian relief response to a
developmental one. It is supplying technical assistance and inputs to
independent farmers working land leased by collective farms. New varieties
of wheat appropriate to different geographic, soil and climactic conditions
have been introduced with very positive production results. As part of the
contribution from USAID, the AKF/PRDP is supporting the completion of
the building of the Pamir I electric power facility.
*       The IRC has supported the rehabilitation and resupply of poultry farms in
West Khatlon.
*       The evaluation of AKF/PRDP (mentioned elsewhere) recommends that
attention be given to the enhancement of livestock and also the potential for
cold water fish, silk and wool production in Gorno-Badakshan.
*       German Agro Action (GAA) plays a prominent role and operates in areas of
Tajikistan where other foreign NGOs have little or no presence. With the
assistance of the European Union and the German Government, GAA has
been delivering foodstuffs and other needed supplies in Khodjent and war
affected areas south of Dushanbe since 1994. It has a large seed distribution,
upgrading and multiplication scheme operating principally in the Garm and
Khodjent regions and is proposing a large revolving loan fund. By the time
current project funding expires, it anticipates having benefited 3,900 farmers
directly and a possible total of 80,000 persons indirectly. GAA is working
with emerging private farmers associations which it hopes will eventually
become multi-purpose organizations with the capacity to extend and supervise
loans, offer technical assistance through extension services and operate a
marketing system.
*       CARE International is actively involved in food security and nutrition. It
completed its Tajikistan Emergency Logistics Assistance Program (TELAP)
to distribute emergency medicines and supplies in mid 1994. Together with
the Center for Disease Control and Prevention, and supported by USAID



- 129 -
funding, it conducted an important study (August, 1994). The study
recommended that CARE focus on food distribution programs in particular
areas of need but also that it assist the development of potable water and
irrigation systems.
*       though CARE appears to have been cautious about moving from relief to
development activities and to working with local NGO formations, it is
proposing to begin a relationship with the Dekhan Farmers Associations by
providing training and agricultural inputs.
18.     Several NGOs have sponsored FFW projects is worthy of note. This fact may be not
unrelated to the prominent roles which USAID and US NGOs are playing in Tajikistan. FFW
projects, in MCI's view, are useful for creating employment in the short term but do not promote
sustainability. Interestingly, the aforementioned evaluation of the AKF encourages that agency
to consider the possibility of supporting FFW projects.
H.      Institutional/Organizational Development and Capacity Building
19.     SCF (U.S.) and Counterpart Consortium (CC) have jointly supported a county-wide
project to build the capacity of NGO local staff in project management and training as well as
establishing the NGO Resource Center. The capacity building began with the training of SCF's
own local staff, but now this service is available to the staff of other NGOs, both foreign and
local. The SCF (US) and CC trainers work as a team.
20.     The AKF, whose Tajikistan projects were recently evaluated, is credited with having
established PRDP, the strongest of the national NGOs in the country in terms of its program and
management capacity.
21.     The AKF is proposing, as well, to work with the Khorog State University to upgrade
some of its programs and also to work with four or five universities to improve English language
instruction.
22.     The Independent evaluation of the UNHCR SED projects concludes that "IRC's
particular strength is capacity building". With this in mind, it proposes that IRC concentrate on
replication of its early projects and development of linking enterprises. (Peduzzi, p. 18) In that
this is an area of the most critical importance, IRC's response to this recommendation bears
further examination.
I.       Health
23.     Some NGOs have specialized in health programs. Though this is not a priority for the
proposed Bank initiative, it is worthy of note that some NGOs have chosen to focus on health:
*       RI has made a substantial contribution in this area. A recent evaluation
found, however, that its future may be compromised because the focus of its
work has been with the government health service which is in a severe state
of crisis due to economic and political conditions.



- 130 -
*       SCF (US) has likewise been involved in health programming but has
emphasized the development, strengthening and training of village health
committees. The same evaluation judged these activities to be a more
sustainable approach than RI's.
*       UNICEF began an emergency program in early 1993. In late 1993 it
conducted a rapid assessment study of the situation and needs of women and
children in Tajikistan. UNICEF's concerns center on the health and nutrition
of women and children. Currently, UNICEF is offering support to a variety
of clinics and hospitals in order to keep as many as possible in operation.
24.     Some of these agencies with a concentration in health, it should be added, have considerable
demonstrated capacity in other areas of programming, some of which fit within the Bank's designated
priorities
25.     It should be noted as well that some of the emerging Tajikistan NGOs are showing an interest
in health. This is most notably true of women's and environmental organizations which are, respectively,
concerned with women's and environmental health issues.
J.      Civil Society
26.     Though most of the international NGO activity in the immediate wake of the civil war was
concerned with emergency relief followed by reconstruction, there are NGOs which have demonstrated
an interest in the question of what can be done to promote the growth of civil society.
*     The Counterpart Consortium, together with the American Legal
Consortium, co-sponsored a regional conference on NGOs and the
Law (October 29 to November 4, 1995). Representatives of seventy-
five (75) NGOs and governments from all five countries attended.
Topics included the relationship between NGOs and government in
a civil society, an appropriate legal framework for promoting the
NGO sector, transparency by both NGOs and government,
registration procedures and tax status for non-profits. The conference
provided and opportunity for country-specific NGO/government
groupings to develop recommendations for legislative reform and
improved administrative procedures.
*     The Counterpart Consortium/SCF NGO Capacity Development
training project has been noted above.
*     The Soros Foundation has stated its intent to support projects to
extend and promote human rights and to develop the media. Soros
does not have its own program but provides support to other NGOs
which have a program development and management capacity. The
Soros Foundation works throughout the former Soviet Union (FSU).
It makes seed grants to enable other initiatives to start. Then it
moves on to other projects.



- 131 -
27.      During the three years it has worked in Tajikistan, Soros assisted IRC, MSF, Relief
International, SCF US and UK and Human Rights International with contributions. Even the UNHCR,
though clearly not an NGO, received an initial contribution from the Foundation towards its work in the
post civil war period.
K.                Analysis of NGO Operating Environment in Tajikistan: Political
28.      From the top levels of Government, there is official receptivity to NGOs bringing relief and
development assistance to Tajikistan - notwithstanding varying degrees of enthusiasm from ministry to
ministry. The Government has responded positively to the idea that the a substantial portion of the
proposed PPAP to be established through the World Bank loan would be devoted to NGO projects that
have the potential for "scaling-up".
29.     However, there has apparently been, it should be added, a recent demonstration of alleged
government interference in the election of a the President of a national NGO, the Tajikistan Red Crescent
Society. (The Red Crescent Society is the largest national NGO with branches throughout the country.
One of its essential principles of operation is independence from the state.) Because of this alleged
interference, the association called an extraordinary general meeting of the members. In order to ensure
the freedom and independence of the meeting, invited official observers of both the ICRC and IFRC were
present. The fact that interference evidently took place in the initial election and that international
observers were brought in must be regarded as a disturbing sign, notwithstanding that a reversal was
secured in this particular instance. An indigenous and autonomous Tajikistan NGO without this kind of
international backing might well have had a more difficult experience.
L.      Legal
30.      Because the legal situation of NGOs in Tajikistan has been reasonably well documented, the
following will simply touch upon some salient features of the law and the regulatory environment.
31.      The principal laws which apply to NGOs are the Constitution of the Republic of Tajikistan,
which recognizes the concept of pluralism and the right of citizens to associate, and the Law of Public
Associations (1991), which defines a public association as "a voluntary unit created by the free will of
citizens who have united together on the basis of common interests". The definition encompasses a wide
range of possibilities from trade unions to political parties, mass movements, associations of women,
veterans and invalids as well as scientific, cultural and sporting organizations and so on.
32.     The breadth of the field of possibilities is both a virtue and a possible problem. Some observers
feel that the law lacks precision and a sense of the differences between one kind of organization and
another. They note, in particular, the substantial distinctions to be made between a political party or a
trade union and a voluntary association. On the other hand, they would not want to see the law become
too restrictive. It is important to note that the Law on Public Associations is currently under review and
amendments are likely to be presented within a few months time.
33.     NGOs, both foreign and local, must register with the Ministry of Justice under the heading of
"public associations". There are now over two hundred (200) "NGOs" thus registered, though only a
small portion these are concerned with relief and/or development, and the majority of those are oriented
to social welfare. There does, however, appear to be a significant number of environmental NGOs,
perhaps reflecting a serious degree of concern about the state of the natural environment.



- 132 -
34.      The information which an NGO wishing to register with the Ministry of Justice must supply
is as follows:
*    its name, purposes and goals;
*    its structure and the territory within which it will carry out its activities;
*    the rules for granting and terminating membership;
*    the rights and obligations of members;
*    the rules for forming governing bodies and structural subdivisions and the terms of
office;
*    its sources of funds and other property;
*    the procedures for amending its charter;
*    the procedures for terminating activity and disposing of remaining property
35.      The registration fees for an international public association amounts to $260 (US); organizations
operating throughout the Republic must pay the equivalent of $80, and those operating in a rayon or local
area, $55. Public associations representing invalids and children are exempt, and associations of women,
veterans and youth pay fifty percent (50%) of the fees applicable. For most foreign NGOs, the fee is not
a problem. Given the amount of cash available to most local citizens and organizations, however, the fees
represent a hardship. Effectively, therefore, a great many local public associations will require foreign
support even to pay their registration fees.
36.      In the event of denial of registration, the right of appeal to the Supreme Court exists.
37.      As regards taxes, the Public Associations Law specifies that Public Associations must pay taxes
on "entrepreneurial activities". Under a decree of the Supreme Council of the Republic, there are certain
exceptions to this rule, including enterprises and institutions related to associations of invalids, museums,
libraries, theaters and so on. Rates of income taxes may be reduced where profits are devoted to
charitable purposes.
38.      In the event of an international treaty whose provisions are different from local legislation, the
former will prevail.
39.     It should be added that organizations operating in a particular region or locality must register
with the department of justice of the particular regional council of people's deputies.
40.      The formal legal requirements for establishing an foreign-based or international NGO in
Tajikistan are minimal. The Ministry of Foreign Affairs, Department of International Organizations and
International Law is the body which registers foreign NGOs. (There is no requirement that local NGOs
register with the Foreign Ministry.) The organization must submit the following under cover letter:
(i)  its by-laws or charter;



- 133 -
(ii) a statement of its goals, objectives and activities in Tajikistan and;
(iii) a written mandate from headquarters for the organization's senior official posted to the
country.
41.      Approval by the Foreign Ministry can be granted very quickly. The foreign NGO then has
access to free consular services and many privileges available to the diplomatic corp. Some NGO
representatives have observed that the Justice Ministry tends to be the controlling face of government,
whereas the Foreign Ministry adopts a more facilitating stance with regard to the registration of foreign
NGOs.
42.      Finally, as regards micro and small enterprise development, there appears to be no legal
problem in organizing and registering businesses. It is worthy of mention here since some of the foreign
NGOs are sponsoring and encouraging micro and small enterprise activities.
M.       Regulatory
43.      It is in the administration of the law where some NGOs observe or experience difficulties. For
instance, as noted above, some have found attitudes held by some Justice officials to be "dirigiste". On
the other hand, a senior official of the same Ministry confirmed to this author that the policy of the
Government is to encourage a vibrant NGO sector which can help to address the severe socio-economic
problems which the country is facing.
44.      The experience of local NGOs in securing the registration of their charters varies considerably.
Some speak of the procedures as being complicated and cumbersome, of having had to wait two years
or more and of having had to submit answers to questions not asked at the outset. There is a feeling that
"city NGOs" with access to money and government offices have an easier time than rural ones, and
especially those which represent the very poor. One NGO representative claimed that officials sometimes
seek bribes. Other organizations, however, appear to have enjoyed a relatively easy process. It may be
that some types of associations, those which represent disabled people for instance, find more ready
acceptance because their categories are specifically recognized in law.
45.      Taxation matters are the source of a good deal of concern for NGOs, both foreign based and
local. Notwithstanding that the Public Associations Law appears to restrict taxation to "entrepreneurial
activities" that might be carried on public associations - and hence profits earned - there does appear to
be considerable confusion regarding how the law is to be interpreted. Several NGO representatives
interviewed in the course of the May, 1996 Mission spoke of their fear that their revenues, regardless
of source or purpose, will be taxed. This has led some to operate on a cash system rather than through
a bank. Whilst most registered NGOs appear not to be paying taxes, this uncertainly is obviously not very
satisfactory, and clarification is needed. The problem appears to be that a distinction between profits
earned from business activity on the one hand and revenues generated from charitable giving and official
donors on the other may not be well understood. There would also appear to be an element of confusion
about national pension plan contributions. This whole area of taxation and related matters is of concern
and will need to be followed carefully.



- 134 -
N.      Successful Project Completion by Sector: Small Enterprise Development
46.     Quite promising results have been reported from small enterprise credit programs. As one
example, the SCF (US) Group Guaranteed Lending Program has had a repayment rate of 100 per cent
(as of mid 1995). During its visit to Tajikistan, the World Bank Mission was informed that MCI expects
a 95 percent (95%) repayment rate on loans it has extended.
47.     A recent Independent Evaluation of the UNHCR supported SED projects found that:
*   the IRC soap making project was economically viable and easily replicable and that the
products were produced in a cost effective and technologically appropriate manner.
(Peduzzi, op. cit.)pp. 8-9) The USAID PVO evaluation also found this project
"impressive". (Wenger et al., op. cit., p. 13).
*   the IRC poultry project farms had the potential to be economically viable by the Spring
of 1996 and replicable, though it raised questions as to their sustainability in light of
inflation. (pp. 10-11) As added endorsement, the USAID PVO evaluation commented
very favorably on this project. (p. 12).
*   the IRC's oil production project is economically viable and profitable as long as cotton
seed and electricity are available. Profitability will be enhanced by the production of
purified oil. In a stable economic environment oil press enterprises should be sustainable.
They should also be replicable. (pp. 12-13)
*    the IRC shoe masking facility is less viable than the soap factory though with low cost
sewing machines and home based production, successful shoe making enterprise should
be replicable. (pp. 14-15)
3   the RI SED Training Program for Village Women evaluation noted that further training
will likely be required to reinforce that already given, that trainees had "demonstrated a
reasonably fair comprehension of the (business) concepts" but that "the biggest obstacle
facing the women is marketing" and that "another possible obstacle . . . is the
procurement of raw materials". (pp. 21-22)
48.     Another study of enterprise development (Pruyne, op. cit., p. 17) proposed to UNDP that this
project be evaluated for potential replication, and if positive, that it be expanded to other parts of
Tajikistan beyond its present base in Khatlon. Without prejudging the outcome of the evaluation, the study
was evidently expressing a vote of confidence in the merits and efficacy of credit schemes aimed at
women.
49.     In the judgement of the US PVO evaluation, some of the most outstanding among the physical
building and rehabilitation projects were:
*     the IRC Water and Sanitation program which put potable water within reach of 40,000
beneficiaries



- 135 -
*      the SCF (US) FFW program which provided shelter for some 100,000 people
0.       Rural Development
50.     As noted above, the AKF/PRDP initiative has been ranked highly by the evaluation
commissioned by World Learning Inc. (the agency administering USAID granted funds for support of
U.S. NGOs working in Tajikistan).
51.     PRDP, with technical assistance from AKF, has placed a high priority on institutional
development to ensure continuity of progress and replicability of results. . . By any objective standard
or comparison of results with similar efforts elsewhere, the performance of PRDP has been outstanding.
(Fort, op.cit., pp. 1-2 and 10)
P.      Capacity Building
52.     The capacity development efforts of the AKF and its local counterpart, the PRDP also scored
very highly by this same evaluation. In fact, the evaluation thought the level of intemal competence has
reached such a high level that the PRDP ought now to redirect its capacity development efforts to
strengthening other local organizations. (Ibid.)
53.     According to another evaluation, the SCF (US) was worthy of recognition. Through its
emphasis on village health committees and its projects which involve local participation in management,
it supports the goal of sustainability. Moreover, it does all of this within a reasonable cost structure.
(Wenger, et al., op. cit., pp. 17-19)
Q.      Reliance on external funding sources
54.     Given the political/economic situation of Tajikistan, it is difficult to imagine there being much
potential, for some time to come, to move away from substantial reliance on foreign funding. Some forms
of program activity, however, do involve less external funding than others. Revolving loan funds, for
instance, maximize local effort and recycle money within the economy.
R.      Project Sustainability
55.     Inflation has been mentioned as a major factor. Political and social stability are key elements
of sustainability.
56.     The 1995 Feasibility Study on Enterprise Development makes the point that sustainability, at
least in the agricultural area, may be enhanced by giving in-kind contributions (seeds, fertilizers, fuel and
the like) with repayment pegged to agricultural output calculated in hard currency.
57.     Another of the factors contributing to project sustainability is the gestation and implementation
periods required. This is an issue that the Bank will need to consider in relation to any expectation of
quick return. An evaluation of the UNHCR's Tajikistan small enterprise development projects is
instructive when it calls attention to the contradiction between quick impact and long term development.
58.     Conflict resolution and peace building are matters of urgent need. Though virtually all NGOs



- 136-
claim to be non-discriminatory as far as employment and other project benefits are concerned, few have
explicit and practiced strategies for addressing social conflict. The USAID PVO evaluation notes,
however, that the SCF (US) has "been among the leading practitioners of the art". The May, 1996 Bank
Mission found a general awareness of the problems of social conflict among NGOs with which it met,
though it did not detect much NGO programming activity in the area. This having been said, there is a
UNDP sponsored pilot project operated by UN volunteers. (An evaluation of the project was underway
at the time of the Mission's visit.)
S.      Staff capacity
59.     There is an abundance of well educated and talented local people available in Tajikistan.
Though most of these are not experienced in the design and management of development projects and
programs, many have found work with NGOs and have proved themselves very adaptable.
60.     Because of the conditions of living and working in Tajikistan, foreign NGO staff have tended
to stay for rather short periods of time, thus contributing to a turnover problem. This issue has been
complicated by the change in role experienced as a result of coming to the country as relief agencies and
more recently having tried to make a transition to development. The staff who are suitable for a relief
operation are often not those most appropriate for a development program.
T.      Transition from Relief to Development
61.     The shift from relief to development is frequently not an easy one for NGOs whose orientation
and experience is in relief programming. Even though these agencies may be extremely professional and
competent in what they do, and though they are able to employ some needed expertise for development
projects, they do not necessarily have the broader institutional awareness and support capacity which more
seasoned development NGOs possess and use to good effect. This experiential gap showed itself in such
areas as public participation in the planning, design and management of projects. Some organizations lack
an intuitive and methodological grasp of how to proceed, simply because it is not part of their experience.
U.      Geographical Limitations
62.     The other impediment is very much related to the foregoing point. Most of the present NGO
projects are to be found in areas where the 1992-93 civil conflict occurred. These, in other words, are
the parts of the country where their experience has been acquired. As a result, NGO relief, reconstruction
and development projects are unevenly distributed throughout Tajikistan. "Scaling-up" through replication
of activities in other areas, and, more especially, starting entirely new activities in new areas, are risky
options and are steps which most NGOs would take very cautiously. The presence of NGOs in certain
regions but not in others is an additional problem when it comes to distributing activities and benefits in
an equitable fashion throughout the country. Moreover, this is a problem which is unlikely to be entirely
solved in the short run.
V.      Factors Contributing to/Constraining Scaling-up
63.     Money is an obvious factor. Stable or unstable economic conditions constitute another set of
factors. If prices of inputs and/or products vary wildly or unpredictably, they negatively affect
investment, production and sales and hinder any consideration of "scaling-up".



- 137 -
64.     The management capacity of NGOs themselves. On the surface, there is no reason to doubt that
the organizations proposing "scaled-up" activities can manage what they claim to be able to do. However,
the ability of each organization to manage and be accountable for projects undertaken is something that
can be addressed closer to the time of actual decisions.
65.     In addition to management capacity are the questions of orientation and expertise. These too
are aspects that need careful assessment when considering potential projects to support. One general
observation is that most of the foreign NGOs operating in Tajikistan came in an emergency relief role
during or after the 1992-93 civil conflict. Some of these are organizations which have evolved over many
years both a developmental and an emergency response capacity and practice. Historically, however,
others have been known almost exclusively as relief organizations. In the Tajikistan context, some have
advanced farther than others in redirecting their programs to development.
W.      Examples of "Scaling-up" Activities
66.     A reading of various reports on NGO activities in Tajikistan would suggest a number of
possibilities. Micro-enterprise development assisted through credit schemes is one such. The SCF (US)
Group Guaranteed Lending Program, for instance, has proved so successful in Khatlon Region that the
agency is considering replicating it in other parts of the country. The AKF Fund for Economic
Development is presently considering the establishment of a small credit scheme to begin in Gorno-
Badakshan and, depending on experience, expand to other parts of Tajikistan.
RESULTS/CONCLUSIONS: MEASURES TO ENSURE EFFECTIVE BANK/NGO
COLLABORATION
67.     Representatives of the foreign NGO community in Tajikistan have, by and large, had no
experience working with the World Bank; the Government of Tajikistan has had little more; and NGOs
and the GOT have, to date, had what might be termed a distant relationship. There will, therefore, have
to be effective means of communication and co-ordination between and among the parties and some
deliberate mutual confidence-building.
68.     Because the proposed loan will be provided from the Bank to the GOT, both parties will have
to agree on the basic terms of the agreement and the purposes for which the money will be spent.
Government, for its part, will have to be satisfied that the projects reflect the best possible national
priorities, and the Bank will need to ensure consistency with its own purposes as well as probity and
accountability.
69.     Notwithstanding the tripartite nature of the relationship which will have to be worked out, there
is much to be said for an "arm's length" project management facility. Indeed, the credibility of the
mechanism, at least in the eyes of participating NGOs and perhaps others in addition, may well depend
on this arrangement. The facility will need to be and be seen to be honest, efficient and competent.
Though it will have to adhere to the policies laid down and agreed upon and be accountable for its
actions, it will need to be free from day-to-day interference.
70.     The possible contradiction between this sort of "arm's length" facility and the desirability of
developing capacity within Government is one that is sufficiently obvious as to require comnment. Given
all the problems of setting up a mechanism within the GOT which would satisfy the tests suggested by
the foregoing point, and which could be able to operate quickly, one is led to the conclusion that this



- 138-
relatively short-term PPAP will need an independent management. Of course, all parties will need to
agree.
71.         Since what is proposed is largely uncharted territory, at least in Tajikistan, experience will need
to be assessed and, if necessary, plans amended. An NGO advisory body could be a very useful device
to provide needed input whilst reassuring the participating organizations.

ANNEX L
REPUBLIC OF TAJIKISTAN
PILOT POVERTY ALLEVIATION PROJECT
KEY INDICATORS FOR MONITORING AND INTEGRATED EVALUATION
INTRODUCTION
1.         An independent evaluation of the Project would assess overall performance against
its objectives of (a) impact on household incomes and (b) capacity building. It would provide a
comprehensive and independent analysis of the results of the projects and the efficiency of the
use of available resources, and derive lessons to be learned. The evaluation is designed so as to
ensure both that its findings are independent and reliable, and also that it is conducted in a
participatory way (involving both implementors and beneficiaries) that will maximize learning.
2.         Such an evaluation has particular importance for a pilot project with the express aim
of charting a path for expanded future investments in poverty alleviation, in a country where the
Bank has as yet little implementation experience. An evaluation, conducted at mid term and
completion, would enable three key things: (i) ensure at mid-term that lessons learnt in the first
Project component are consolidated and incorporated in the second; and (ii) provide a "lessons
learned" context for the preparation of a follow-up project based on the second component, and
(iii) provide important pointers for future more specialized sector work including infrastructure,
health, education and micro-credit.
3.         The overall evaluation would verify and integrate the results of separate evaluations
of the individual components and sub-components of the Project (although its scope would not
necessarily be restricted by them), and on the results of monitoring incorporated in each
component. In order to allow this to happen, key indicators need to be established that can be
measured in a compatible fashion across both components of the Project at three points in time:
baseline, mid-term and end-of-project. For this reason, the evaluation is termed "integrated".
The remainder of this annex covers the three related topics of key objective indicators, the
conduct of the integrated evaluation, and monitoring indicators.
OBJECTIVE INDICATORS
4.         Key objective indicators would be measured and maintained by the Project in order
to track, in a consistent and comparable fashion, the essentials of performance against objectives.
The key objective indicators are summarized below in Table 1, and discussed in the following
paragraphs. The first Project objective of impact on the incomes of the poor would be measured
across all sub-components by five key indicators, of which two would indicate the extent to which
this objective has been met in a cost effective and participatory fashion. In addition, each sub-

component would be evaluated according to three specific indicators relating to the strategic entry
point selected (e.g. shelter, micro-credit, agricultural production of female-headed households,
agricultural reform), that address in particular aspects of impact, coverage and sustainability. The
second Project objective of capability building would be measured by two general indicators
across all components, with a third relating specifically to the training of TASIF staff. Thus each
sub-component would be evaluated on ten indicators, of which seven are common to the Project,
and three specific to the sub-component. An additional capacity-building indicator is included
for the second component. These indicators, and the specific measures and instruments to
compile them, would be refined in a pilot baseline exercise .
A.    Poverty Impact Indicators
5.    It is difficult in situations such as Tajikistan to measure household incomes reliably,
owing to the importance of non-monetary items. The social assessment pointed to three key
proxy indicators that were highly sensitive to household poverty levels and appear suitable for
generalization across all sub-components (Annex F). Food security and nutrition is the single
best measure of household economic status and purchasing power, food being the largest budget
item for poor households. Food insecurity needs to be measured in its dimensions of incidence,
depth and seasonality. The nutritional status of children can be measured by standard rapid
survey techniques, such as MUAC (mid upper-arm circumference) used extensively by UNICEF.
(Two sub-components whose design permits the direct measurement of household incomes on
a small scale are identified in the next section).
6.     Clothing, especially shoes for children, is the second priority of poor households,
particularly in areas subject to winter cold.
7.     Shelter and fuel is the third key household incomes proxy indicator. The availability of
domestic fuel for cooking, and for heating during the winter months, is a critical concern of poor
families.  Local scales should be constructed for standards of overcrowding in sleeping
accommodation, together with a simple score for shelter quality (leaky roof, no windows, no
flooring etc.). An indicator of household durables (beds, mattresses etc.) might be added.
8.     A common or compatible calculation of cost-effectiveness would show  cost per
beneficiary for certain types of specified or measured benefits.
9.     An indicator of community participation would show the scale and intensity of local
involvement in the planning, implementation, financing and monitoring of programs. Community
participation is seen primarily as an approach rather than an objective. However, a participatory
approach was an important criterion for the selection of programs for scaling up in the first
Project component and is expected to be a prominent feature of TASIF microprojects in the
second component. Moreover, the close relationship between local powerlessness and poverty
revealed by the social assessment points to community participation in poverty alleviation
activities, together with institutional capacity-building (discussed below), as being an important
indicator of social capital formation.



- 145 -
B.     Specific program indicators
10.    The performance of each program would be evaluated according to three key indicators
specific to the objectives of that program. While each program would have its range of additional
indicators for monitoring and individual internal evaluations, the three specific indicators are
selected primarily to measure the aspects of coverage, delivery of the specific benefit, and longer
term sustainability.
11.   The shelter program has as its main objective to assist 2,000 returned families with
shelter in five districts. Beneficiary selection would ensure the inclusion of the neediest and most
vulnerable groups, including female headed households and ethnic minorities.  Locally-
manufactured tiles would be used in place of asbestos sheeting. The three specific indicators
therefore measure (i) the quantity of shelter improved as a result of the project and the number
of households benefiting, in proportion to the overall identified need (ii) the inclusion of poor and
vulnerable categories of households as beneficiaries; and (iii) the adoption of tile technology.
12.    The micro-credit program aims to extend a group savings and credit guarantee scheme
to 3,750 poor women in 250 groups, and to build institutional capacity. The three specific
indicators would measure (i) the active loan portfolio, comprising the number of beneficiaries,
the loan volume and the repayment rates, indicating the extent to which poor women are able to
access the benefit; (ii) the change in household incomes as a result of the project, and (iii) the
creation of productive micro-enterprises as a result. The measurement of household incomes,
impractical for application across the Project, is feasible within the restricted context of this
program by virtue of its regular monitoring, and will allow testing of simple and inexpensive
methods of investigating household budgets. The creation of productive micro-enterprises is an
important indicator of the future sustainability and replicability, showing the productive use of
household credit as investment, rather than for short-term consumption needs. The important
component of institution-building is captured by a general indicator.
13.     The Female-Headed Households program would provide support for household food
production to 3,700 female-headed households to improve food security and incomes. The three
specific indicators selected are (i) the number of participating households; (ii) household
incomes, for the same reason as in the micro-credit program, and (iii) food productivity. The
range of institutional and group formation benefits, including community day care centers,
schools and access to local services would be captured either in the first specific indicator, or in
the general indicators of community participation and institutional capacity.
14.    The agricultural reform program aims to increase food crop production and productivity
in six of seven districts in Gorno-Badakhshan, by bringing additional land into cultivation through
small scale self-help irrigation channels. This would benefit 1,600 resource-poor farming
households. The three specific indicators would measure the (i) the land development potential
(length of channels constructed and potential command area realized); (ii) actual land development
(number of hectares developed for cultivation, number of households benefiting; and (iii) actual
increase in food production and productivity by family and unit area. The evaluation would take
into account that the land development schedule extends for several years beyond the life of the
Project, and that therefore the benefit stream for some may commence after Project completion.



- 146-
C.    Capacity building indicators
15.    The second Project objective of capacity building is to strengthen in country capacity to
develop and implement poverty alleviation programs through (i) development of TASIF as a
central organization to fund and provide technical support for microprojects; and (ii) through the
first Project component, to build up local community, NGO and local government capacity to
initiate and organize poverty alleviation programs at local level.
16.    Three indicators would be used to measure and evaluate Project performance and the
achievement of this objective: (i) the development of national and local institutional capacity to
implement participatory poverty alleviation programs; (ii) the number of national and local
government officials trained in project identification, proposal preparation and community
participation, together with the number of proposals received by TASIF, and (iii) the number of
TASIF staff trained in these skills. The first two of these indicators are common to both
components of the program, while the third is specific to the second component.
17.   The institutional capacity indicator is both qualitative and quantitative, and must be
measured at several levels:
A.    Each of the programs in the first Projct component has a specific local
institutional objective which is both a means and an end: participation through
community-based organizations and associations of beneficiaries. The work
groups of the shelter program, the micro-credit groups and umbrella NGO, the
associations of female headed households and the managements of day care
centers and schools, the local farmers' groups negotiating irrigation channels, all
play an important part in empowering the poor and marginalized to organize to
improve their conditions.   They help rebuild civil society and ensure
sustainability of process and impact in poverty alleviation. The indicator at this
level would measure scale (size, geographical and social coverage), scope and
institutional strength. The design of an instrument and a method to capture this
aspect objectively, in the context of the diversity of organizations emerging will
be a challenge for the baseline evaluation to resolve.
B.    At the second tier, the institutional capacity of local government officials, and
local or national NGOs to target, appraise, implement and monitor poverty
alleviation programs can be determined by simple institutional assessments on a
common checklist.
C.    At the third tier of TASIF itself, the key measures of capacity would be results
against its central objective, the ability to channel investments through
community microprojects for poverty alleviation, and to ensure and account for
the results.
18.   The second key capacity building indicator is trained officials. This indicator would
capture the number and geographical distribution of national and local government officials and
NGO officials trained in project identification, proposal preparation and community participation.
The indicator would measure training effort (person-days, cost) against intended results, such as



- 147 -
the number, quality and distribution of proposals received by TASIF, or changes in knowledge,
attitudes and practice. Measured across the Project, this indicator would capture the extent to
which the four NGO programs, and TASIF itself, have contributed lessons, demonstration,
training for replication of their successes over a wider geographical and institutional canvas.
Additionally, each training event should have a simple internal evaluation as standard quality
control practice.
19.    The third key capacity building indicator is training within TASIF itself, the only
indicator in Table 1 is not measured "across the board". As with the previous indicator, this
indicator should measure not only training effort but training results in terms of knowledge,
attitudes and practice that contribute to the organization's mission and performance.
20.    The above set of indicators would facilitate and provide a sound foundation for an
integrated and overall evaluation and should therefore be emphasized in monitoring. The
integrated evaluation, however, would not be limited or determined by these indicators.
MONITORING INDICATORS
21.    Each program has specified its own monitoring indicators, which have four main
purposes: management of implementation, triggering disbursements, tracking impact, and
providing a foundation for subsequent evaluation. The range of these indicators, and how they
would be measured, is shown in Table 4 at the end of this Annex.
D.    Establishing a baseline (May 1997)
22.    To assess the Project's overall impact requires a baseline study, that would allow the key
objective indicators in Table 1 to be measured in a compatible fashion across the Project's several
programs. The first of two main steps to accomplish this is to develop, through workshops and
pilot field exercises, instruments and procedures to operationalize these indicators. By the time
of Project approval [April-May 1997], measurement of the key indicators in Table 1 would have
been pilot tested through a workshop and field exercises that would bring together the NGOs, the
TASIF monitoring and evaluation officer and relevant local consultants or research organizations
under expert technical assistance. The indicators would be not only expert derived, but would
include indicators selected in consultation with local commnities and capable of being measured
by them;
23.   Having agreed and tested measurement of the indicators and constructed a suitable sample
frame6', quantitative and qualitative baseline data would then be collected and analyzed by the
four NGOs and by TASIF for the first batch of 10-15 micro-projects, to establish quantified
measures. The inclusion of TASIF in the baseline exercises would improve learning from the
first to the second Project components, and facilitate the setting up of a fuller beneficiary
assessment for later TASIF micro-projects.
6/ A 10 per cent sample of direct beneficiaries, plus a control group of suitable size, seems appropriate.



- 148 -
MANAGEMENT OF INTEGRATED EVALUATION
A.    Objectives
24.    The integrated evaluation would determine the extent to which the Project had achieved
its objectives, and particularly evaluate the following aspects, without necessarily being limited
by them:
A.    design (needs identification, selection of intervention strategy, targeting)
B.     social and geographical targeting
C.     impact
D.    sustainability (of process and of impact)
E.     community participation
F.     cost effectiveness, relating the identified benefits to resources invested
G.    replicability
B.     Design and method
25.    The evaluation has been designed in order to ensure the following features: (i) the
independence, multi-disciplinary professionalism, integrity and reliability of its conclusions;
(ii) the effective participation of beneficiaries and implementors; and (iii) the generation of
maximum learning and capacity building. Any tension between the first objective (independence
and integrity) and the second (participation of implementors and beneficiaries) would be resolved
by ensuring a collegial approach under an independent leader.
26.    A three-week mid-term evaluation would be conducted about June-July 1998, linking and
coordinating the findings of the separate evaluations of the four NGO programs comprising the
first Project component. It would verify through field visits the validity of key monitoring
indicators and the essential accuracy of the quarterly progress reports, and review the training
materials under preparation. The progress of the TASIF MIS would be assessed against these
findings, with particular emphasis on (i) assisting with the identification of simple and effective
monitoring and evaluation instruments, and the inclusion of community-based indicators; and (ii)
the design of the TASIF microprojects beneficiary assessment. The design and selection of
microprojects would also be evaluated. In reviewing Project progress against key indicators, the
mid-term evaluation should pay particular attention to an initial institutional capacity assessment
of TASIF, in order to allow time for recommendations to be taken into consideration during the
second year of the Project.
27.    The focus of the mid-term evaluation would be on lessons learned and Project course
correction. TASIF, local communities and NGOs would all have the opportunity of mutual
learning and education.
28.    A final four-week evaluation would be conducted in June-July 1999, combining the
evaluations of the four NGO programs, a selection of the TASIF microprojects, the beneficiary
assessment, and an institutional assessment of TASIF. The evaluation team would make site
visits to a selection2' of TASIF microprojects to verify monitoring and reporting procedures
1   A 20 percent sample of projects is recommended. It would be important to ensure a representative range of examples
of success or difficulty in implementation.



- 149 -
against observations. The central questions to be addressed, on the basis of an analysis of key
indicators, are: (i) the justification of continuing or expanding investments in poverty alleviation
through participatory micro-projects; (ii) the lessons learned that would maximize the cost-
effective impact and sustainability of such investments; and (iii) the capacity of TASIF to manage
such investments effectively.  The final evaluation would also relate its findings to the
expectations and judgments of the PAD, so as to derive lessons for future project preparation.
C.    Management and inplementation
29.   The evaluation exercise, both mid-term and final, would be contracted competitively by
TASIF, subject to no objection from the Bank, to a suitably experienced and qualified lead
individual or organization who has not been involved in project design, preparation or
implementation. The evaluation leader would, in consultation with TASIF and the implementing
NGOs, assemble a multi-disciplinary team of eight well-qualified local and international experts,
ensuring a sufficient range of expertise to cover the project content: poverty and its alleviation,
construction, micro-credit, gender, agriculture, institutional capacity and MIS training,
community participation. Of the team, three or four international members would be proposed,
one by each of the international NGOs, and paid for from their program budget. At least four
local counterparts would be selected by the team leader and paid for from TASIF's budget.
Interested relevant donors might also be invited to participate.  TASIF's Monitoring and
Evaluation Officer would actively assist the team with research documentation and analysis.
30.   The evaluation team would work as an interdisciplinary team, reviewing each of the
programs, and the TASIF microprojects, with full access to all project sites, personnel and
documentation, and the ability to conduct their own spot field research. The team leader would
be responsible for using all of the inputs and reports from the team members, and would have
full responsibility for the end-product.
31.   In addition, the team would be expected to constitute local evaluation teams for brief
participatory, on-site evaluations. These local teams should include community representatives,
local government officials, other knowledgeable or local persons, and representatives of the
implementing agency.
32.   The team would have access to all relevant TASIF and NGO records. and would be
expected to ensure physical visits to project sites in each region of the country.
D.    Outputs
33.   The consultant would produce a report in Russian and in English for each of the three
evaluation components that would be confidential to TASIF, the implementing NGOs and the
Bank. TASIF and the implementing NGOs would ensure dissemination of relevant findings to
participating communities.
34.   These reports would be made available in final form one month after the evaluation.

ANNEX 0
REPUBLIC OF TAJIKISTAN
PILOT POVERTY ALLEVIATION PROJECT
PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION SCHEDULE
1.    The Pilot Poverty Alleviation Project (PPAP) would increase the incomes of some of the
poor in Tajikistan and improve their access to essential economic and social services. This would
be done by (a) scaling up selected existing NGO programs that are having a positive impact on
poverty alleviation, and (b) developing the Tajikistan Social Investment Fund (TASIF) as a
demand driven, cost-effective and transparent funding mechanism that would respond to proposals
from the local level to carry out small investments in social and economic infrastructure. It would
also finance the implementation of 70 microprojects throughout Tajikistan.
Implementation Arrangements
2.    The executing agency for the PPAP would be the newly established TASIF. The TASIF
would be created by Presidential Resolution with the necessary autonomy and authority to enable
it to execute the project according to the Legal Credit Agreement. The TASIF would operate
according to a detailed Operational Manual which would provide information on the organization
and structure of the TASIF. A draft of the Operational Manual would be prepared by TASIF
prior to Project Effectiveness based on those used in other social funds and input from the World
Bank and technical assistance. The Operational Manual would detail the implementation
arrangements for microprojects as well as the responsibilities of the TASIF in monitoring,
supervision and reporting on the NGO programs supported by the PPAP.
3.    The TASIF would enter into service contracts with each of the NGOs to be supported
through the PPAP. Attachments to the service contracts would stipulate the procedures,
benchmarks and monitoring indicators to be followed by the NGOs for implementation of the
NGO programs. Currently the TASIF Executive Director and the leadership of the NGOs are
finalizing the details of the proposals and reaching consensus on the delegation of responsibilities
for financial management, monitoring and reporting.
Procurement and Disbursement
4.    Given the nature of the project, it is difficult to estimate of procurement and disbursement
procedures and schedules. These would be discussed with the World Bank mission and the NGOs
during the forthcoming appraisal mission. The procurement arrangements for the NGO programs
would be specified in attachments to the service contracts. Procurement arrangements for the
microprojects and purchasing of equipment, furniture and consulting contracts would be specified
in the Operational Manual.



- 169 -
Financing
5.    The total project costs are estimated at US$11 million, of which IDA would finance
US$10 million. The Government of Tajikistan and communities would contribute to the costs as
well.
Project Accounting and Monitoring
6.    By project effectiveness, the TASIF would have hired staff and acquired equipment and
technology to enable it to monitor and account for the use of PPAP funds. External auditing
would be conducted on an annual basis. A beneficiary impact assessment would be undertaken
to understand the impact of the project on poor communities.
Completion of the PIP
7.    The TASIF will complete its Project Implementation Plan by appraisal of the project. The
TASIF will specify critical factors that could affect the success of the project, the role of the
World Bank during project implementation, and a detailed schedule of implementation
activities.
